PSYCHOLOGICAL INTRODUCTION It is almost always the highest forms of human activity, will, resolve, free will, which have been studied by philosophers. We were naturally interested in the manifestations of the activity that it was most useful to know in order to understand the conduct of men, their responsibility and the moral value of their actions.

    but, although this way of approaching the question is perhaps the most natural, it is nevertheless the most difficult and the most dangerous: the highest and most important phenomena are far from being the simplest; on the contrary, they present many modifications, accessory developments which prevent a clear understanding of their true nature. The most elementary facts, as well in psychology as in the other sciences, are sought today preferably, because we know that their knowledge easier to acquire will clarify much that of the more complex forms. This humane activity in its simplest, most rudimentary forms, which will be the subject of this study. PIERRE JANET This elementary activity, either that it has been observed in animals, or that it has been studied in humans even by alienist physicians, has been designated by a name that must be kept, that of. automatic activity. This name, indeed, even according to its sensetymological (aùxo <;, even [jiktoç, effort, from [xioiiai or [jiato [jiat, to seek, to strive, Littré) seems to apply quite well to the characters presented by these actions. We designate, in fact, under the name of automatic a movement which has two characters. It must first have something spontaneous, at least in appearance, take its source in the very object which moves and not come from an external impulse; a mechanical doll which walks alone will be said to be an automaton, a pump which is made to move outside cannot be one. Then, it is necessary that this movement remains

    however very regular, and is subjected to a rigorous determinism, without variations and without whims. Now, the first efforts of human activity have precisely these two characteristics: they are provoked and not created by external impulses; they leave the subject itself, and yet they are so regular that there can be no question about them of the free will demanded by the higher faculties.

    but we usually add to the word automatic another meaning that we do not accept so readily. An automatic activity is, for some authors, not only a regular and rigorously determined activity,

    but still a purely mechanical activity and absolutely without consciousness. This interpretation has been the source of many confusions, and many philosophers refuse to recognize in the human mind an automatism, which is

    however real and without which many phenomena are inexplicable, because they appear only to admit automatism is to suppress consciousness and reduce man to a pure mechanism of extended and insensible elements. We believe that we can simultaneously admit both automatism and consciousness, and thereby give satisfaction to those who observe in man a completely determined form of elementary activity, like that of an automaton. and to those who want to preserve for man, even in his simplest actions, awareness and sensitivity. In other words, it does not seem to us that, in a living being, the activity which manifests itself outside by movement can be separated from a certain form of intelligence and consciousness which accompanies it inside, and our aim is to demonstrate not only that there is a human activity deserving the name automatic,

    but also that it is legitimate to call it a psychological automatism. The philosophers who have regarded activity as a psychological phenomenon,

    but who have examined it only in its most perfect manifestations, have very clearly separated it from other phenomena of the mind and have considered it as a particular faculty distinct from intelligence and sensitivity. Without a doubt, the complicated phenomena which have acquired, in consequence of their development, a multitude of precise characters are clearly separated from one another, and it is certain that it would not be legitimate to confuse an abstract reasoning with a practical resolution.

    but these faculties, so different when they are completed, do they not approach each other in their origin and do they not start from a lower form of life and consciousness where activity, sensitivity and intelligence absolutely merge? This is what we believe we can establish, and the study of the elementary forms of activity will be for us at the same time the study of the elementary forms of sensitivity and consciousness. Another character always attributed to higher activity is the character of unity: voluntary power seems one and indivisible like the person himself of whom it is the manifestation. It is impossible to understand human actions if we want to represent all activities on this model. Unity and systematization seem to us to be the end and not the starting point of thought, and the automatism we are studying often manifests itself in multiple feelings and actions independent of each other, before to give way to the one and personal will. It is this remark which enables us to establish the general divisions of our work. We will first study automatism in its most simple form when it is complete and it occupies the whole mind, that is to say when we do not observe in the mind of ' a person with only one thought and only one automatic action.

    but we will then have to admit that, in many cases, automatism can be partial and occupy only a part of the mind, when several elementary activities can develop simultaneously in the same thought. Finally, human activity sometimes presents itself in abnormal forms, incoherent and convulsive movements, unconscious acts ignored by the very one who accomplishes them, impulsive desires contrary to the will and which the subject cannot resist. These irregularities are inexplicable if we only know the theory of free will and one. Do they become more intelligible by examining lower forms of activity? The study of these abnormal activities will allow us to complete and verify the solutions given to the previous problems. The method which we have tried to employ, without claiming to have succeeded in any way, is the method of the natural sciences. Without bringing any preconceived opinion on this problem in advance, we gathered by observation the facts, that is to say the simple actions that we wanted to study; we have only formulated the necessary hypotheses with regard to these well-observed facts and, as far as possible, we have verified by experiments the consequences of these hypotheses. Research of this kind cannot be done by means of personal observation of the facts which take place in our own consciousness. Indeed, the phenomena that it presents to us can only with difficulty be the object of a regular experimentation; they are then much too complicated and they take place in the midst of very numerous and difficult circumstances to determine, last

    but not least they are always incomplete. Consciousness does not make us know all the psychological phenomena which take place in us; it is an indisputable truth today that we hope to confirm again. It is from this that arise the most serious difficulties which psychologists have encountered when they have wished to confine themselves to personal observation by consciousness. When we want to demonstrate that there are “uniformities of succession between states of the mind,” in short, when we want to make psychology a science analogous to other sciences, one is stopped by this difficulty: “it is that in the series of associations, at every moment one comes up against unconscious representations'”. As, for many authors, an unconscious phenomenon is only a ‘Lange phenomenon. History of maleialism. Traduct., 1877, II, 427. Physiology, it is to physiology and its laws that we ceaselessly appeal to explain the phenomena of the mind. This often useful appeal sometimes seems premature to us, because, on the one hand, psychology gives up finding true laws of spiritual phenomena and, on the other, physiology simply observes the coincidences between such and such a moral fact and such a fact. physical and does not actually explain the laws of consciousness. Stuart’Mill, when he maintains against Auguste Comte the legitimacy of a scientific psychology, ^ only responds in an embarrassed manner to this difficulty; it is indeed insoluble if we admit as phenomena of consciousness only the incomplete facts furnished by personal consciousness. To have simple, precise and complete phenomena, we must observe them in others and appeal to objective psychology. No doubt we only indirectly know the psychological phenomena in others and psychology could not begin with this study;

    but, according to the acts, the gestures, the language, one can induce their existence, just as the chemist determines the elements of the stars according to the lines of the spectrum, and the certainty of the one of the operations is as large as that of the other. Our study on automatism will therefore be an attempt at experimental and objective psychology. One of the great advantages that the observation of others presents over personal observation, is that one can choose the subjects that one studies and take precisely those which present in the highest degree the phenomena that one wishes to examine.

    but individuals who thus present in an exceptional degree a phenomenon or a character which will be hardly apparent in a normal man, are necessarily sick. This has, I believe, no disadvantage. It is necessary to admit for the moral this great principle universally accepted for the physical since Claude Bernard, it is that the laws of the disease are the same ones as those of health and that it does not There is in this only the exaggeration or the reduction of certain phenomena which were already found in “elk-this.” If one knew well about mental illness, it would not be difficult to study normal psychology. Moreover, from another point of view, “man is only half known if he is observed only in the healthy state; disease does as well ‘Stuart Mill. Logic. Traduct., 1880, II, 433. part of its moral existence than of its physical existence * ”. It is not bad that psychology penetrates a little into the details of the various moral disturbances, instead of always remaining in generalities which are too abstract to be of any practical use. VS’ This is why an experimental psychology will necessarily be in many points of view a morbid psychology. All experimentation supposes that we vary the phenomena and the conditions in which they present themselves: the disease does indeed effect some of these modifications for us,

    but too slowly and under imprecise conditions. We only have real psychological experiences if we artificially modify the state of a person’s consciousness in a predetermined and calculated way. Moreau (de Tours), one of the most philosophical among the alienists, claimed to arrive at this result by means of the intoxication procured by hashish. “It was,” he said, “an excellent means of experimentation on the origin of the foliée ”While sharing this desire for psychological experimentation that Moreau (de Tours) is one of the first to express, I hardly appreciate the process he used. Having witnessed, only once it is true, a intoxication produced by hashish, I found that the physical disturbance caused by this substance was very serious and very dangerous for a rather meager psychological result. Moreover, the moral modifications thus obtained are very little at the disposal of the experimenter and cannot be directed by him. So this method of psychological experimentation is in reality impractical. On the contrary, it is a state which is easy to provoke and which is not dangerous, in which the moral modifications are obtained very easily and which Moreau would have preferred to any other if he had known it well, it is the state of somnambulism provoked. Already Maine de Biran, one of the precursors of scientific psychology, in his new considerations on sleep, dreams and somnambulism, insists on the advantage that psychology could draw from the study of these phenomena: he was interested to the Broussais experiments. De Virritation et de la folie, 26. ^ Moreau (de Tours). Da hashish and mental alienation, 30. meetings of the magnetizers of his time, he followed their sessions and spoke about them frequently. Later M. Taine also indicates the use of somnambulism in psychology; we also know the works of Jouffroy, Maury and many other psychologists on this subject. The magnetizers insisted on the advantage that could be drawn from their procedures: “By giving us the means of making the various cogs of thought function separately, of reducing their exercise to its elementary operations …, by teaching us moreover to draw from their latency an entire class of ways of being faculties of the soul, Braidism provides an experimental basis for psychology which from then on becomes a positive science and takes its place within the framework enlarged animal physiology ^. However, little justifiable prejudices, the fear of this renown of charlatanism which had remained attached to the operations of animal magnetism, for a long time prevented these advice from being followed: it took all the work and all the discoveries of contemporary scientists whose names are well known to put beyond doubt the existence of nervous sleep and the advantages that science could derive from its study. We will not discuss here the reality of sleepwalking nor the danger of simulation, this discussion would be long and above all banal, because it is found everywhere very well done; we think, moreover, with Dr. “Despine who has studied sleepwalking a lot, that” to easily consider things as fraudulent is a convenient opinion in order to dispense with studying what one does not understand. ^ “A few precautions are enough, which each experimenter must know how to take himself, depending on the circumstances, to be on his guard against attempts at overgrowth, rarer, in my opinion, than is generally believed. Also, without insisting on this point, we will only say under what conditions we used hypnotic sleep for our research. The subjects on which these studies were made were almost all, with the exceptions which we will point out, Taine women. De Vintelligence, 1878, 1, 5. - D '’ Philips (Durand de Gros). Theoretical and practical course in Braidism or nervous hynotism, 1860, 169. ^ D “‘Despine. On somnambulism studied from a scientific point of view, 1880, 57. Affected by more or less serious nervous diseases, particularly of this disease very variable which we designate under the name of hysteria. These neuroses, having as their principal character a great mental instability, offer us, and by the natural accidents which they cause and by the predisposition to sleepwalking that they generate, the field most favorable to experimental studies of psychology and especially to studies on automatism. However, subjects of this kind present special difficulties in their study. They are extremely variable, and without even speaking of that inclination to deceit which is attributed to them with some exaggeration, they are not always in the same physical and moral dispositions. It is necessary to follow them for a long time and with a lot of attention, “to study them not for a moment

    but in all the phases of their disease *” to know exactly in what circumstances and under what conditions one experiences. Then, because of their mobility, they are very easily subjected to all external influences and change very quickly according to the books that are allowed to them to read or the words that one recklessly pronounces in front of them. Because of this character, it is impossible to make any valuable experience with them if we study them only once, at random, without knowing exactly their disease state, character, previous ideas, etc. It is also impossible to ascertain any natural fact, if one asks them in public, if one indicates to people present the experiments which one makes and the results which one awaits. They have to be studied often and you should always experiment alone or with competent people, knowing the issues in advance and aware of the necessary precautions. These are the conditions which we have tried to fulfill in the research which we are going to present. They focused on fourteen hysterical and hypnotizable women, on five men suffering from the same disease, on eight other individuals suffering from insanity or epilepsy. The number of subjects could easily have been increased if we had been anxious above all to experiment only on well-known subjects whose physical and moral condition could be fully determined. Garlic- * Despine. Op. Cit., 322. Their it was not necessary to cite separately the experiences made on all these subjects: several, being identical to each other, do not teach us anything new and their names and their characters would unnecessarily complicate our work. exhibition. It seemed preferable to us, where possible, to repeat most of the experiments on a small number of subjects which, once well known, would be cited in preference to others. That is why, except in specific cases, most of the reported facts have been described according to four main subjects which we refer to by first names rather than by letters, Léonie, Lucie, Rose and Marie ^ These four people, more than all the others, seemed to us to satisfy the conditions of a good psychological experiment. Studied for a long time, they were perfectly known in all the details of their disease and their character; examined with care and only by competent persons, they have been modified as little as possible by examples or by reckless words. None of these precautions could have been taken and even all these studies would have been completely impossible, if we had not been supported in this work by the people most able to make it succeed. Studies of moral man can hardly be done today by philosophers without the help of those who have devoted themselves to the study of physical man. Without the doctor who shows him the subjects suffering from the particular diseases he needs, who protects him against possible accidents and constantly lends him the help of his experience, the psychologist could not approach the experimental study of the phenomena of consciousness. . Although this association, so to speak, of physicians and psychologists is today quite general, I cannot refrain from saying how much I I found encouragement and help from the doctors in Le Havre. I especially wish to express all my gratitude and affection to M. le D Gibert and M. le ‘In order not to embarrass our psychological studies with accessory descriptions, we will bring together in an appendix the few biographical and medical details which ‘It is useful to know about the main subjects that we mention. From “Powilewicz who, without hesitating in the face of the embarrassment and annoyance that such research could cause them, kindly took part in all my work. If the observations reported in this book, more perhaps than the theories which are supported there, may have some value or some interest, it is to them that the main merit should go.Le Havre., December 1888. PART ONE TOTAL AUTOMATISM CHAPTER ONE ISOLATED PSYCHOLOGICAL PHENOMENA Gondillac, when he undertook to analyze the human mind, devised an ingenious method to clarify and somewhat simplify the complex phenomena which present themselves to consciousness. He supposed an animated statue capable of experiencing all emotions and understanding all thoughts,

    but having none at first, and, in that absolutely empty mind, he wanted to introduce each sensation one after another and in isolation. . It was an excellent scientific method. The multiplicity of phenomena which intersect in the universe prevents us from discerning their relations, their dependencies; with a stroke of a magic wand, let us suppress all these phenomena and in this absolute vacuum let us reproduce in isolation a single fact. Nothing will be easier then than to see the role and the consequences of this phenomenon; they will develop before us without confusion. This is the ideal method of the sciences; Gondillac hoped to apply it to the mind. Unfortunately, this theoretically beautiful method was completely impracticable, for the philosopher did not have the statue of which he spoke and did not know how to reduce a consciousness to its elementary phenomena. So he made his experiment in imagination, instead of questioning nature and waiting for the answer, he himself made the questions and the answers, and he substituted for the analysis he boasted of doing. a completely artificial construction. Well the experience that Gondillac dreamed of and that he could not try, it is possible for us today to carry out it almost completely. We can have before our eyes real living statues whose minds are empty of thoughts and, in this consciousness, we can introduce in isolation the phenomenon whose psychological development we want to study. It is thanks to a sickly condition known for a long time by doctors,

    but little examined by philosophers, that we will find this statue. It is the nervous disease most often referred to as catalepsy that will provide us with these abrupt and complete suppressions, then these gradual restorations of consciousness which we want to take advantage of for our experiments. “Catalepsy,” says Saint-Bourdin, one of the first authors who made a precise study of this disease, is an affection of the brain, intermittent, afebrile, characterized by the suspension of the understanding and the sensibility and by the aptitude of the muscles to receive and to keep all the degrees of contraction given to them ‘. »[This definition, without being perfect, gives a fairly correct general idea of ​​a disease state which occurs naturally, in some predisposed individuals, following a shock or an emotion and which one produces. artificially in some subjects by various well-known methods. There is no need to concern ourselves, at least at the beginning of this work, with the origin of this state; we can say of catalepsy what M. Ballet said of speech disorders: “It will not matter to us whether this or that speech or writing disorder is produced by a tumor, a focus of softening, a toxic agent. The wheels of a watch, said Buzzard, can be stopped by a hair as well as by a grain of sand, and the disorder which arises then always remains the same, whatever the cause which produced it. We can thus examine the psy- ^ Saint-Bourdin state. Treatise on catalepsy, 1841, 7. - Ballet. Internal language, preface, xv. chemical produced by this disease, regardless of its origin. No doubt a person suffering from catalepsy will not have the ideal simplicity of the statue of Condillac: the condition will be more or less perfect, and its interpretation will always raise problems.

    but a real experience, even if it presents some obscurity, it is worth a hundred times better than a simple theory,

    but imaginary. Let us begin, then, according to our method, by describing this state and its most general characteristics; we will then review the various interpretations which are possible and the hypothesis which seems to us the most probable. Finally, returning to experience, we will verify the consequences of this hypothesis by the details and the varieties that this disease state can present. Thus we will have described and interpreted a state in which, as Condillac said, the phenomena of consciousness present themselves, we believe, in a state of isolation. I DESCRIPTION OF THE PHENOMENA CAUSED DURING THE CATALEPTIC STATE It is unfortunate to begin our description of the states in which the psychological automatism is shown by the description of a condition which is quite rare, and by experiences which cannot easily be repeated. However, this should come as no surprise: we want to study very simple phenomena early on, and nature always presents complex things. Nothing is more complicated than a normal mind, nothing is more complicated also than an ordinary madness or a fit of hysteria. We are forced to choose rare phenomena if we want them to be simple. Also, although we have observed by ourselves a fairly large number of persons afflicted with those nervous diseases in which the cataleptic state may present itself, we have never ourselves witnessed an absolutely complete crisis of natural catalepsy; the ones we saw n ' were only imperfect varieties. We have only collected the description of two natural crises, one observed in Paris at the Hospital de la Pitié by my brother Jules Janet, the other which was produced by a thunderbolt on a subject that I knew,

    but which I could not see at the time. I was able to observe artificial catalepsies more frequently,

    but on only three subjects. Catalepsy could sometimes be provoked in Lucie by suddenly showing her a bright light of magnesium, or by compressing her eyes slightly during sleepiness. Catalepsy occurred naturally at times during Rose or Leonie’s induced sleepwalking. It was also produced,

    but at the last only, when, during sleepwalking, we opened his eyes to the light. All the other people I studied had only varieties of the condition called somnambuli.:me, or catalepsies so transient that one could, as has often happened, be overlooked. It is therefore necessary to make the description of this state according to the few artificial catalepsies which we have been able to examine,

    but a few quotations will show that they do not differ in their essential features from natural catalepsy. Whatever the means employed to produce the catalepsy, let us examine the aspect which the subject presents then, and choose as an example Leonie’s catalepsy when it is quite complete and comes closest to the classical description. first and most apparent character, it is the absolute immobility of the subject. Never a normal person goes several minutes without any movement; some movements of the hands, the eyelids, the lips, some slight quivering of the skin, always manifest the activity of thought and the feeling of external things. Léonie, on the contrary, in the state which we describe, invariably retains the attitude in the- ‘Descriptions of catalepsy are found in a great number of works, I quote here only those that I have been able to know and consult. : C.-E. Saint-Bourdin. Treatise on catalepsy, 1841. P. Baragnon. Study of animal magnetism from the point of view of an exact practice, 1853, 226. Delasiauve. Treatise on Epilepsy, 1854, 263. Despine. Scientific study on sleepwalking, 1880,194. Axenfeld. Treatise on neuroses, 2 “ed., 1883, 908. Bottey. Animal magnetism, 1884, 29. Paul Richer: Hijstéro-éjnlepsie, 1885, 610, 668, 775. CuUerre. Magnetism and hypnotism, 1886, 124. Binet and Y era. Animal Magnetism, 1887, 114. What catalepsy surprised her, without the slightest tremor coming to reveal consciousness and thought. The eyes themselves wide open, without any blinking of the eyelids, steadfastly hold the same direction. In short, the movements of organic life, the beating of the pulse and the respiration, stand alone, and all the movements which depend on the life of relation and which express consciousness are suppressed. If we do not intervene and especially if we refrain from touching the subject, this state persists without any modification for a more or less long time: natural catalepsies have been seen to last for days and artificial catalepsies to last for several hours. In the subjects I have been able to study, this state never lasts long and does not last more than a quarter of an hour; it changes naturally and ceases to present this character of absolute moral inertia. As long as the subject remains cataleptic, various experiments can be carried out on him which lead us to observe important characters. These characters, which are hardly more than the consequence of the inertia previously pointed out, can be reduced to four principal ones which we will describe briefly, because they are all well known. 1 ° Z “continuation, the persistence of all the modifications which one can produce in the state of the subject. - If we touch the limbs, we notices that they are extremely mobile and, so to speak, light, that they offer no resistance and that they can be moved very easily. If we abandon them in a new position, they do not fall back according to the laws of gravity, they remain absolutely motionless in the place where we left them. The arms, legs, head, trunk of the subject can be put in any position, even the strangest; so we naturally compared these subjects to painter’s mannequins that are folded in all directions. Even Léonie’s face is likely to be modified in this way: if you open your mouth, raise or lower your eyebrows, the face, like a wax mask, can be molded and retains its new expression; in others, the muscles of the abdomen themselves keep the imprint of the hand ^ We did different * Paul Richer. Hystero-epilepsy, 292. studies of great importance on these cataleptic attitudes. We have seen with precision devices how much these postures remain invariable: instead of trembling, as the outstretched arm of a normal individual always does and very quickly, the limbs of these people remain in the air for a long time without moving. ; instead of producing an acceleration and a modification of the respiratory rhythm, as always happens in normal humans, this tiring position of the arm does not in any way change the slow movement of the chest. It is only at the end of a rather long time, an hour and more, according to some authors, twenty or twenty-five minutes, according to the others, that the arm begins to descend because of fatigue or muscular wear,

    but this descent takes place very slowly and very regularly without these jolts and oscillations which are observed in normal humans. As Léonie’s catalepsy did not last more than a quarter of an hour, I did not observe this descent which would probably have started a little later. These “poses” are one of the best known phenomena and the most characteristic of natural catalepsy, as these few observations prove. Here is an excerpt from a description of Laënnec and Maisonneuve reported by Saint-Bourdin: “… He speaks to her, she does not hear; he touches her, she does not seem to feel him; he lifts an arm to him, his arm remains in the position in which he put it; they raised the patient upright, bent the neck, lifted one leg, everything kept the given position. Here is another from Saint-Bourdin: “She kept the same attitude she had at the time of the attack: if she was standing, she remained there; if she went up the degrees, she had a high leg [to go up and during the whole time of the catalepsy, she kept this same attitude. During this state, raising one of her arms, bending her head, putting her on one foot with her arms outstretched, were she placed in any position, provided the body had been brought into balance, she kept perfectly until the end was the last attitude he had been given. “It is true,

    however, that natural catalepsy does not always exhibit that flexibility which is almost constant in artificial catalepsy. “In others’ Paul Richer. Hystero-epilepsy, 614. “Saint-Bourdin. Op., Cit. 46.” Id. Ibid., 6i. sick, the body is in such a state of rigidity that, if we push them, they fall without changing their attitude *. We will have to come back to this difference; let us only observe that the stiffness, the apparent contracture of the limbs still retains here a characteristic and properly cataleptic aspect. The contracture is not general, that is to say, it does not invade all the muscles at the same time in the same way and to the highest degree; for there would then be a special attitude always the same, well described in the attack of tetanus or in certain epileptic seizures: the body would be stretched out, bent backwards, the limbs extended, the wrists along the body and flexed in, the fists closed, etc. The muscles on the contrary are contracted to different degrees, so as to give the body an expressive attitude, as in the case of Saint-Bourdin cited above where the body stiffened in the attitude of prayer, the knees bent and the hands joined, could be overturned without changing posture. This detail is important in distinguishing catalepsy from true general contracture. Another modification that can be imposed on the cataleptic limbs is movement. Instead of giving up the arm in a state of stillness, we make it oscillate two or three times and let it go in the middle of the movement: the oscillation persists as before the position persisted. We can thus communicate to the arms, legs and head of this mannequin, a movement which will not stop before the end of the attack. The same character is still found, though perhaps less often reported, in descriptions of natural catalepsy, “A five-year-old girl having been deeply shocked at one day that her sister had removed a chosen piece during the meal. which she herself wanted, suddenly became stiff. The hand which she had extended towards the dish with her spoon remained in this state; she looked askance at her sister with indignant eyes; although he is called aloud and excitedly excited, she did not hear; she neither moved her mouth nor her lips, she walked when pushed and led with the hand …. ^ ”One could also quote among cataleptics persistence of Saint-Bourdin. Op. Ciï., 53. “Observation of Tissot, reported by Saint-Bourdin. Op. Cit., 9. rERRE JANET 9 sensations or even images; Léonie or Lucie remain indefinitely with their eyes fixed on a light which ‘we have shown them;

    but these phenomena being difficult to study on catalepsy, we will find them later with more clarity. Those which we have pointed out suffice to verify this first characteristic of catalepsy: the continuation, the persistence of catalepsy. all the modifications imposed on the subject. 2 ° Limitation or repetition. - Instead of touching the subject, let’s face him in the direction of his gaze and make a movement ourselves instead of moving his limbs. Slowly Léonie will move and put her arm, then her whole body, exactly in the position we have taken. This phenomenon has received the name of specular or mirrored mitation, because the subject ordinarily imitates with his left arm the movement which we make with the right arm and resembles our own image in a mirror. The fact is not,

    however, absolutely general, for, if Leonie imitates in this way, Lucie, in these same imitations, does not reverse attitudes; it is true that his catalepsy is much less complete. Instead of exerting an action on the subject’s sight, one can impress his hearing, at least in appearance. We N’ we will not study this phenomenon on Léonie who hardly presents it,

    but on Rose, in whom it is completely complete. If I speak out loud next to her while she is in a cataleptic state, she repeats exactly my words with the same intonation. This fact has received the name of echolalia or echoing speech. He is very curious; the subject, changed, so to speak, into a phonograph, repeats all the sounds which strike his ear, without appearing in the least affected by the meaning of these words. Usually the noises are repeated with the mouth,

    but in one case, the D’Powilewicz, then present, having clapped his hands, Rose repeated the noise, clapping his hands as well: echolalia mingled here with rhyme. 3 ° Generalization or expression of phenomena. - Most often the modifications imposed on the subject remain partial and affect only one member;

    but sometimes, when the cataleptic state is quite complete, they show a tendency to become generalized and to affect the whole body. Jules Janet observed a natural cataleptic which always repeated with his left arm what was done with his right arm and vice versa. It is the phenomenon of synkinesis that I observed only in Léonie and again for certain acts only. If I clench one fist, the other closes as well. If I raise a hand to him in front of his face in the position of prayer, the other hand takes the same position and comes to be placed against the first. The acts which complement each other are with her, as we see, known and habitual acts. These same habitual acts are likely to become much more generalized and cause a change in the whole body of the subject. This is one of the best known, the most popular, so to speak, phenomena of catalepsy, for it always produces a quite extraordinary spectacle. We see the figure, the whole body come to life, harmonize with the attitude of one of the members and take on a striking expression of reality. Have one of Leonie’s fists been closed, the other also closes, the arms rise in the attacking position, the body rises, the face changes; the tight lips, the closed fists and the furrowed eyebrows express only anger. Did I put one hand extended near the lips, the other hand also places there and seems to send kisses, the figure suddenly changes and, instead of expressing fury, lips and eyes, everything is smiling. We can indefinitely change these attitudes, these plastic poses and make the subject express love, prayer, terror, mockery, always with equal perfection. To switch from one attitude to another, it suffices to slightly modify one of the gestures of the body; with Léonie, it is even enough to touch the muscles of the face. MM. Charcot and Paul Richer succeeded in modifying the attitude of a cataleptic by making one of the muscles of the face contract by the electric current. With Léonie, the figure is cataleptic like the rest of the body; it suffices to raise his eyebrows so that they remain as we put them and that they bring the attitude of terror throughout the body; the expression is no less violent, although it is provoked by such a futile cause. ^ ° Association of states with each other. - Until now, the subject has done nothing on its own, never left the state in which ‘Paul Richer. 0; j. c / <., 669. it had been placed; it is now necessary to note cases where the scene being performed is much more complete and more developed. I put Lconie’s hands in the attitude of prayer and the face takes on an ecstatic expression. I left it in this state, because I intended to wait how long the expression would hold. I see her getting up from the seat where she is sitting and very slowly taking two steps forward. At this moment, she bends her knees,

    but always with a singular slowness; she kneels, leans forward, her head bowed and her eyes raised to the sky in a marvelous ecstatic posture. Will it stay that way and, the attitude being completed, keep the cataleptic immobility? No, here she is getting up without my having touched her, she lowers her head further and puts her joined hands in front of her mouth, she takes five or six steps even more slowly than earlier. What is she doing? Here she is now, making a great respectful bow, kneeling once more, raising her head a little and, eyes half closed, opening her lips. What she does is understandable now, she is going to receive Communion. Indeed, the communion made, she gets up, greets again, and, with her head completely bowed, returns to kneel in its original position. This whole scene, having lasted a quarter of an hour, is then interrupted by the end of the cataleptic state. It is the most complicated act that I have seen performed during catalepsy; we see that it is composed of successive phenomena which have provoked each other, instead of being only, as before, the continuation of the same modification. We must therefore compare other acts provoked by the intermediary of such or such sense and which all have the character of being composed of successive actions and different from each other. If cheerful music is played in front of the subject, he laughs, then begins to dance; sad music makes him cry. If we put a piece of thread in Léonie’s hand, she makes the gesture of threading a needle, then begins to sew. If you put a pencil in her hand, she makes the gesture of writing,

    but only bars indefinitely; if you put an umbrella in her hand, she opens it, puts it over her head, etc. “A natural cataleptic, studied by Forestier, eagerly ate (vorabat) whatever was put in his mouth.” »We find in ‘Saint-Bourdin. Op. Cit., 30. The works which deal with catalepsy a large number of these complex and associated acts. I only wanted to briefly recall those cataleptic phenomena which are all well known,

    but which seem to me of capital importance at the beginning of a study on automatism. As M. Gharcot said: “In true catalepsy, there is absolute moral inertia … ; it is by this, in good method, that the study of hypnotic suggestions should be commenced. »II MECHANICAL OR PHYSICAL INTERPRETATION OF THESE PHENOMENA Can the phenomena which we have just described be of interest to psychology? This is the first question one should ask when considering cataleptics. These motionless women, like statues, without resistance of any kind and without words, do they still think, do they still have some consciousness which brings them closer to us? It is permissible to doubt this and to wonder if the organic life which seems to subsist alone is not sufficient to explain all the phenomena observed. This is the explanation one would find in Haidenhain’s works. He explains the cataleptic movements by reflex actions of the lower centers of the brain, actions which do not reach the higher centers where consciousness develops. It was also to this opinion that the English alienist Mandsley was attached. Finally, it is the doctrine which we find most fully expressed and defended in the works of Dr. Despine. ^ This author refuses to recognize any kind of consciousness, not only during catalepsy,

    but even during sleepwalking. All the acts performed during these abnormal states seem to him to be purely “organic”, analogous to those which the heart and the lungs constantly do without our knowledge. They are indeed automatic acts,

    but " automatic acts of the medulla, the bulb, ‘Churcol. Maladies du si / stème iierveux, 1881, III, 337. - D '’ Despine. Natural psychology, 1868, I, 490 et sq. Scientific study on somnambulism, 1880. of the hemispheres ”, because“ we must not say unconscious and automatic activity of the mind, that is contradictory; we must say the unconscious activity of such or such a nervous center ”. “Carpenter is wrong,” he adds, “to call these sensori-motor acts ideo-motor, because there is no sensation or idea there, there are no real acts. automatic minds ”; finally, “to ask psychology for an explanation of somnambulism would be wrong; physiology alone can give this explanation * ”. As our aim in this work, if we are not too ambitious, is precisely to demonstrate the contrary, we must insist on the study of the opinions of Dr. Despine which seem to stop our work from the start. If we applied this thesis of absolute unconsciousness to sleepwalkers, as the author himself does, it would be absolutely unbearable. To pretend that a person who speaks, solves problems, spontaneously shows sympathies and antipathies, acts as he pleases and often resists our orders, has no more consciousness than a mechanical doll, is to go up well behind Descartes’ famous animal-machine theory. Because the conscience of a sleepwalker is much more obvious than the conscience of a dog and no one today doubts the conscience of a dog.

    but, applied to cataleptic states, this theory does not fail to have some force, and, as we must always put the theories we want to discuss in their best light, it is by taking this last point of view that we will study the thesis of Dr. Despine. We hope to show that, even in the latter case, his arguments are not sufficiently demonstrative and leave the field open to other assumptions. Dr. Despine’s arguments, scattered amidst a large number of sleepwalking studies, which are all, it is interesting to note, psychological studies, can be classified into two groups: 1 ° Most proofs are drawn from the fact of oblivion which characterizes the phenomena of somnambulism and especially those of catalepsy: “we designate by conscience,” says the author, “* Despine. Sleepwalking, 80. knowledge, self-perception, by the being who feels himself to be, of what happens in his personality, of his own acts, of himself; this work will only deal with this **consciousness**. )) From such a definition of **consciousness** it follows that if there are acts that the ego does not attribute to itself, that it does not recognize having done, these acts should not have been aware. “When it is a matter of a serious act, capable of impressing feelings to the highest degree, if the individual who performed it completely ignores this act, it would be against nature to attribute this ignorance to oblivion. It can only be explained by the non-participation of the ego, of the personal **consciousness** in this act, which is due entirely to the unconscious psychic activity, that is.. Now there is no state after which this forgetfulness is more characteristic than after the cataleptic state. Somnambulists have sometimes been able to retain part of the memories of their actions;

    but the cataleptics wake up from their attacks convinced that nothing abnormal has happened. What is more, if, instead of waking up completely, the cataleptic subject only passes into another abnormal state, apparently not very different, like the somnambulic state, he does not preserve the memory of the previous attitudes and movements either. “This ignorance can only find its explanation in the non-participation of the ego in what the body has done, the cerebral activity which characterizes the ego, the conscious personality having been paralyzed ^. This same trait is found in individuals who have been subjected to inhalations of ether or chloroform. Whatever words the patient said, “his ego, his conscious being had not participated in everything that had happened, for the patient, who soon came to himself, claimed to have felt nothing, to ignore completely that he had been operated or bandaged, that he had spoken the words and that he had performed the acts; the violent reactions of which one spoke to him, these various phenomena were thus purely automatic. As the state of a cataleptic or somnambulist individual is infinitely similar to the state of a chloroformed individual (the demonstration of this point forms one of the most interesting parts of the work ‘Despine Somnambulisme, 98. of D '’ Despine), we can conclude from one to the other. Chloroform suppresses sensitivity and consciousness, and that is precisely why it is used; since sleepwalking presents the same characteristics and in particular the same forgetfulness, we must believe that it brings about the same unconsciousness. It suffices to go through these discussions and others of the same kind, 011 forgetting is always accepted as a proof of unconsciousness, to be struck by the f-‘agility of this demonstration. Is it therefore impossible that truly conscious acts are forgotten? This forgetting would be inexplicable, says Despine, when it comes to sleepwalkers. Either, it will be necessary to seek the reasons for this forgetfulness, which will perhaps be very difficult to find;

    but, even if we could not always explain it, forgetting to a thing which has been really conscious is nonetheless a possible thing and very often real. If, as an English author says% a Times reader is suddenly killed after reading, he will certainly have no memory, must we conclude that his entire reading will have been unconscious? We can never then admit the conscience of any man, and not even our own; because nothing assures us that tomorrow an accident or an illness will not come to erase the memory.

    but let us admit for a moment, which seems inadmissible, that forgetting is sufficient proof of absolute unconsciousness, is it quite certain that there is no memory of cataleptic phenomena? It is true that, at least for the subjects I have studied, there is no There is never any memory when they return to the state which by convention is called the waking state or the normal state.

    but a certain recollection is first manifested in the following catalepsies by the habit which the subject rapidly acquires of performing with more perfection the acts which he is made to do more often. Then, and this is more important, there are in these same individuals certain psychological states, certain somnambulisms, since this is still the agreed name, where the subject perfectly recalls the memory of catalepsy. “You put your hands on me like that,” Leonie said to me, like ‘Gurney. The problems of hypnotism., Proceedings of Ihe soctely for ^ sychical research. II, 282. If I played the flute, you closed my fists, etc.long ignored. We will resume the study of these somnambulisms * later; it was good to know right now that there is a memory of catalepsy.

    but this memory, Mr Despine will say, exists only in another abnormal state which itself is a state of unconsciousness. “If his paralyzed conscious activity does not have the knowledge of the time spent during the access, because he has not received any fingerprints, the automatic activity which participated in these acts has retained the imprint, and the memory can reappear in another access ^. Thus, then, the memory, if it reappeared in the normal state, would be a good proof of consciousness;

    but, since it reappears in another state, it is only a proof of physical automatism. Does this not prove that recollection is neither a proof of consciousness nor of unconsciousness and that we must seek outside memory for indications of the condition of the cata- leptics. 2. The second group of proofs on which Dr. Despine relies to conclude that there is no consciousness during sleepwalking seems to be drawn from the dialogies that this state presents with certain phenomena of insensitivity. Some rather complicated acts are performed under conditions such that they seem not to be felt by the person performing them. Let us not dwell on doubtful cases. “An apoplectic struck to death, without coming out of the coma in which he was plunged, took his watch from the bedside and struck the hour with the air of profound attention,” w This observation does not prove much, for on the one hand this individual, at the time when he performed this act, was not yet dead, and had perhaps (no one can prove the contrary) some remains of consciousness and, on the other hand, as he died soon after, he could never tell whether or not he had felt what he was doing. In a very interesting chapter, the author enumerates all the acts accomplished by a ‘Cf., same part, ch. ii, § 3. * Despine. Ibid., 99. decapitated frog, a newt cut in half, by the sections of the praying mantis, etc., and he shows unceasingly that these acts perfectly resemble those which the conscious intelligence commands in other cases by the same devices,

    but that they must be done without consciousness now, because the organ necessary for consciousness has been removed. “This intelligent power manifested by the lower section cannot derive from an ego, from a being who feels himself to be; otherwise there would be two separate beings in this animal: one for the upper section, which can act intelligently, and the other for the lower section. However, this is not admissible in the current state of science ^. We will answer: why then is this inadmissible? The absolute unity of the ego is a metaphysical conclusion, perhaps true,

    but which must result from the facts and not be imposed on them. You have no other proofs of the consciousness of the animal than the intelligent adaptation of its movements. It is necessary to see if this intelligent adaptation reveals to us one or two or three consciousnesses in him and not to conclude until later on his unity or his division. I like the intelligent acts that the author borrows from the normal life of men best when they have their intelligence and speech intact and can assure us themselves that they have no feeling for these acts. The most interesting are the usual acts which M. Despine describes in a very curious way. He insists on the unconscious character of habit: it is not the intelligence which retains a piece of music and which performs it consciously; the artist must have his piece “in his fingers, in his mouth”. “When I’m looking for a pattern that I don’t remember,” said one of them, " I let my fingers wander over the keyboard and they find it immediately; they have better memories than I do. Moreover, if the artist consciously thinks about what he is doing, he will be less successful, “the mistakes committed come more often from the mind than from the automaton”. This same unconsciousness, already so remarkable in habitual acts, is found in all the acts we perform; we are not aware of all the delicate work that the muscles have to do when we raise our arms or when we open our mouths; “The ego controls the movement and it is a power independent of it and completely organic which coordinates the muscular action necessary for the execution”. Finally, the purely organic acts, those of digestion, respiration, etc., Do they not constantly manifest the most wonderful intelligence that the ego not only does not know,

    but is not always even capable of understanding. These are a few examples of the very numerous acts which take place in us, without us, which are accomplished without the participation of the ego, consequently without any consciousness, and which must be related to the purely organic functioning of the marrow and the brain. . The acts performed by cataleptics and somnambulists are nothing more marvelous; they are words, coordinated movements, which are very similar to those we do out of distraction or out of habit, without knowing it. These acts must therefore have the same nature, and since the former are unconscious, so are the latter. I don’t I will not insist on the description of these unconscious acts borrowed from normal life: the author describes the details with real psychological talent, all the more curious as he refuses these facts to have any psychological character.

    but I will allow myself to make some reservations on the interpretation of these phenomena. Let us admit that the actions performed in catalepsy (for that would be very inaccurate for acts performed in somnambulism) strongly resemble, if not to organic phenomena,

    but at least to habitual acts. Should we admit without hesitation the absolute unconsciousness of habit? The pianist Despine speaks of can play his piece by heart without paying attention to the movement of his fingers,. Other facts, on the contrary, remain conscious, even though habit has made them faster or easier. “Thus the phenomena of memory, the awakening of ideas under the influence of association are incontestably the results of habit; they are nevertheless carried out with consciousness. “What is lacking in the usual phenomena to be perfectly known” See, on this subject of the unconsciousness of habit, a study by ML Dumont. Revue philosophique, 1876, I, 326. = Ibid., 328. by us, it is therefore attention rather than conscience, and, when we ignore them or that we think we ignore them absolutely, nothing proves that ‘they do not have a consciousness of their own; the slightest effort of attention will make manifest louse? ' us a consciousness of habitual acts that we did not create and that already existed previously.

    but, it will be said, there are acts of the body that are completely unconscious for us, like the acts of organic life. Either, although, in reality, the cataleptic act of communion hardly resembles the beating of the heart or the digestion. It would now be necessary to prove that these unconscious acts for us are unconscious in themselves. “The stimulation of the soft palate by the alimentary bolus or by a foreign body,” says M. Gh Richet 1, “produces either swallowing or nausea; there appears to be some sort of vague discernment of the nature of the irritation. It is a rudimentary psychological character, a sort of discernment of the marrow. This is the case with all the reflexes which everywhere show a sort of sensitivity and discernment, although we are not aware of it. A large number of physiologists have recognized this role of elementary consciousness. Buffon attributed to the coordinated organic molecules in the animal body species of material sensations foreign to thought and to the ego. Gh. Bonnet attributes the ability to smell to all parts of the body and to the plants themselves. Pfluger, Auerbach, Lewes, and many others attribute sensitivity and sometimes intelligence to all the nerve centers. Discussing all these theories, perhaps adventurous, would be pointless and would take us too far;

    but their statement suffices to make it understood that a habitual or even organic act does not is not necessarily oblivious because it is ignored by me. Also the assimilation of cataleptic acts to such phenomena, even though it would be indisputable, does not prove their absolute unconsciousness. In reality, we never directly know a single consciousness, it is our own when we feel it; all other consciousness is known only by an induction or a supposition. No one will ever be able to demonstrate mathematically that the person speaking to me is not a mechanical doll with articulated language, and the Cartesians reasoned rigorously when they said of a wounded dog: cries out and feels nothing, s In this question of the conscience of others, as in many others, we must stick to the likelihoods and probabilities. Now, we ordinarily suppose the existence of consciousness from two signs, speech and intelligently coordinated actions. The first sign, the word, is considered the most decisive, and that is right;

    but it is only a more complex and more perfect case of the second, a set of movements more complicated and more intelligently coordinated than the others, and, if this first sign leads us to suppose consciousness, the second leads us on the same assumption, perhaps with a little less probability. The cataleptics do not speak, this is true, and we will have to come back to this important fact later,

    but they act intelligently. If I put on the outstretched arm of a cataleptic a weight of two kilos, the muscles of the arm and those of the whole body tighten so that the arm supports the weight without flexing. If I put a needle in his hands, all the movements coordinate in a different way than if I put my hands in prayer. There is adaptation, unity of movement, in a word, what is ordinarily considered as a sign of intelligence.

    but, it will be said, coordination, intelligence and even sensibility can exist without consciousness. “Several very complicated, intelligent acts, reaching a goal that is perfectly determined and varied according to the circumstances, acts resembling exactly those that the ego commands … can be automatic *” (that is to say here unconscious). “The man,” said Maudsley, in the same sense, would not be a worse intellectual machine without consciousness than with it ^. In short, consciousness is only an accessory, an epiphenomenon whose absence does not disturb anything. I do not know why this theory has been attributed to M. Ribot, who,

    however, with excellent arguments, had protested against it. hardly understand it; it does not seem to me * Despine. Psychology, I, 491. * Ilerzen. The Brain and Cerebral Activity, 1887, 212. ^ Ribot. Diseases of the Person, 16. intelligible neither from the psychological point of view nor from the physiological point of view. What do we mean when we speak of “the reasoning of the medulla and the intelligence of the brain *”? Nothing else except that there is another consciousness than ours in the marrow or in the brain, for reasoning without consciousness has absolutely no meaning. On the other hand, if we admit that consciousness results from a set of physiological conditions leading to a certain act, we cannot admit that another time this exactly the same set again leading to the same act is given without counseling. The same conditions would sometimes be causes of consciousness and sometimes not. The fact of consciousness seems to us on the contrary very important in the series of organic phenomena: its presence or its absence, as we will see more and more, considerably modifies things. When we know that a complicated phenomenon, such as the movements of anger or the gestures of prayer, can only exist in us with a set of conscious emotions and ideas, we have no right to assume that the exact same gestures occur during catalepsy without being directed and unified by some consciousness. So we will suppose, which is now legitimate at least as a hypothesis, that the cataleptic phenomena are psychic phenomena whose nature it remains for us to determine. What is now only a hypothesis will be verified, we believe, more and more by other phenomena of the same kind. III PSYCHOLOGICAL INTERPRETATIONS. - CATALEPSY ASSIMILED WITH SOMNAMBULISM The acts performed during catalepsy are dependent on psychological phenomena: here is a proposition which seems very simple,

    but which is susceptible of very different interpretations. For psychological phenomena are by Nathan Despine. Somnambulism, 85, extremely varied tare and it is not irrelevant to explain the facts which we have described by one or the other. I will not speak of an easy interpretation, which was in fashion for a long time. It consisted in connecting all the facts that one did not understand to a voluntary and perfectly conscious simulation. It is a completely false idea to believe that a psychological or even imaginary disease is always a simulated disease, and besides catalepsy is of all the abnormal phenomena the one which can be the least simulated.

    but, without relating catalepsy to a complete intelligence calculating its tricks, we can explain by a semi-intelligence understanding the thoughts of the operator, realizing his actions, without having the strength to oppose it; in short, we can compare catalepsy to somnambulism and explain all these acts by suggestion. “In order to put a limb in cata- lepsy, it is not necessary to open the subject’s eyes, nor to subject him to a bright light or a violent noise, as is done at the Salpêtrière; it is enough to raise this member, to leave it some time in the air, if necessary to affirm that the member can no longer be lowered; he remains in suggestive catalepsy: the hypnotized person whose will or power of resistance is weakened passively retains the imprinted attitude we can compare catalepsy to somnambulism and explain all these acts by suggestion. “In order to put a limb in cata- lepsy, it is not necessary to open the subject’s eyes, nor to subject him to a bright light or a violent noise, as is done at the Salpêtrière; it is enough to raise this member, to leave it some time in the air, if necessary to affirm that the member can no longer be lowered; he remains in suggestive catalepsy: the hypnotized person whose will or power of resistance is weakened passively retains the imprinted attitude we can compare catalepsy to somnambulism and explain all these acts by suggestion. “In order to put a limb in cata- lepsy, it is not necessary to open the subject’s eyes, nor to subject him to a bright light or a violent noise, as is done at the Salpêtrière; it is enough to raise this member, to leave it some time in the air, if necessary to affirm that the member can no longer be lowered; he remains in suggestive catalepsy: the hypnotized person whose will or power of resistance is weakened passively retains the imprinted attitude as is done at the Salpêtrière; it is enough to raise this member, to leave it some time in the air, if necessary to affirm that the member can no longer be lowered; he remains in suggestive catalepsy: the hypnotized person whose will or power of resistance is weakened passively retains the imprinted attitude as is done at the Salpêtrière; it is enough to raise this member, to leave it some time in the air, if necessary to affirm that the member can no longer be lowered; he remains in suggestive catalepsy: the hypnotized person whose will or power of resistance is weakened passively retains the imprinted attitude. All this is perfectly correct, and we will study, during somnambulism and during waking, what M. Bernheim calls suggestive catalepsy and even its different varieties;

    but we are dealing here with a collection of phenomena brought about by different processes, or better still by the disease itself, and which, while being psychological, present quite different characters. Catalepsy and somnambulism are only degrees of each other, that is incontestable, and we will see many intermediaries between them;

    but A difference of degree is not a zero difference, especially when it comes to moral phenomena. Let us therefore try to specify the degree to which the consciousness of cataleptics stops. 1 ° The immobility and inertia of the subject are much greater in this state than in any other: a normal person or a sleepwalker, especially with his eyes open, moves much more spontaneously. This spontaneity is noticed ‘Bernheim. De la suggestion, 1886, 94. - Cf. Liébault. Sleep, 412. in the execution of acts, even of ordered acts or suggestions. Not only can there be often very great resistance and independence, which never exists in any degree during catalepsy,

    but also there is variety, changes in the execution of the same acts. A sleepwalker does not always perform the same act in the same way; sometimes she does it quickly, sometimes slowly, sometimes with good humor, sometimes protesting, sometimes in one way, sometimes in another. On the contrary, nothing equals the regularity of cataleptics: no change of character, no external impression which distracts or modifies them; their gestures, their steps are always mathematically the same Léonie will always take the same number of steps in front and to the right to go to Communion, and she will run up against a wall without moving forward rather than turning left. A sleepwalker who will always be able to adapt his actions to circumstances therefore shows a completely different intelligence. 2. The preceding difference is doubtless only a difference of degree, although it is easily appreciable;

    but here is the difference of degree in intelligence leads to the presence or absence of an important character. One of the most important signs of catalepsy, although negative, is this: the subject cannot speak. He is not’ it is not about the articulate speech which it possesses when it repeats the sounds in the echolalia, it is about the language as sign of the thought. The cataleptic does not answer questions either by word or by any sign. Rose, in certain deep sleeps, had her mouth more or less paralyzed,

    but she answered me with a sign of the hand which meant “yes”, or another which meant “no d. When she has a moment of catalepsy during the hysterical crisis or during sleepwalking, she does not respond to me at all with any sign, although she has nothing paralyzed, that she can echo or repeat gestures. . To take an example, I suppose that we take these two women, Rose and Marie, in a state where they are on the outside quite identical, stretched out, eyes closed, motionless,

    but one, Rose, is in a cataleptic attack (for there are catalepsies with her eyes closed), the other, Marie, in simple sleepwalking; I approach each one successively and I pronounce aloud, in the same tone, the same sentence: “Did you sleep well last night?” Rose, without moving, repeats in the same tone: “Did you sleep well last night?” Marie suddenly turns around, smiles and says, “Not too bad, thank you,

    but I had a bad dream. Am I wrong to conclude that these two women, perhaps identical in appearance, are not in exactly the same psychological state *? If the cataleptic does not use speech, it is because she does not understand it. This is easily verified by trying to give orders to these subjects through speech. We can shout in all tones: “Raise your arm”, Léonie does not move, she does not seem to hear; Rose keeps repeating, “Raise your arm,”

    but neither of them raise their arms. It is true that I find myself here in contradiction with M. Paul Richer; this author, although he has noticed that some cataleptics do not obey oral sugges- tion ^, nevertheless writes: “While B is in a cataleptic state, we catch his eye and, the leader on the ground, we says she is in a garden full of flowers. As soon as the cataleptic state ceases, she makes a gesture of surprise, her physiognomy comes alive: “How beautiful they are,” she says, and, stooping, she plucks flowers, makes a bouquet, ties one up. to her bodice, etc. ^, ..> For me, a subject behaving in this way is no longer in a cataleptic state. It is not there, it will be said, that a question of words and names; undoubtedly, the different states through which the human intelligence can pass form such a continuous series that it is impossible to trace precise divisions therein, and such and such a subject will find himself in intermediate states which one can may call indifferently from one name or another.

    but if we attribute the name of cataleptic to a subject who understands verbal suggestions and who speaks, there is no longer any difference between catalepsy and somnambulism. Indeed, all the other symptoms are either found in all hypnotic states, or else, like the paralysis produced ‘Cf. Binet and Féré. Animal magnetism, 1887, 210, and CuUerre. Magnetism, 162. ‘Paul Richer. Op. Cit., 781. Id. Ibid., 697. PIERRE JANET 3 by friction of the tendons *, do not have enough generality, since I have not been able to observe them on any subject. It matters little, moreover, whether one designates the state I have described under the name of first somnambulism or state of complete suggestibility; the only important thing is to understand well the psychological modifications of the subjects in this state, because there are absolutely only psychological differences to distinguish all the states. Well ! consciousness, which exists here as everywhere, for it disappears, I believe, only with life, is, in this state, more rudimentary than in any other. This consciousness is capable of sensations,

    but incapable of ideas; able to hear,

    but unable to understand. It should not be concluded that the one can speak at random in front of cataleptics without any danger for future experiments; they can retain words even without understanding them and if, as we will see later, this memory awakens in a later more intelligent state, then it will be understood and will have its suggestive power.

    but the only thing certain is that the words are not understood now, and that it is not intelligent obedience that manifests in catalepsy. As a result, while appearing extremely inert and docile, the subject is in reality unwieldy and obeys his own inspirations much more than those of the operator. If I show Leonie performing the communion scene that I have described, it will be believed that she is obeying a command given by me. In reality, I do I had not ordered or even planned what she was going to do, and the first time I was very surprised. I know from experience now that by putting the hands of this subject in a certain position and then leaving it for a few minutes, I’m going to bring up the fellowship scene.

    but even now I am not directing this scene; if I wanted to change it in the least, make the subject go to the left, for example, or make him kiss a crucifix before communion, I would not succeed. If I speak to the subject, I am not understood, and if I touch his body, I simply stop the scene; I am therefore a simple spectator rather than an actor. It is from his own background that the subject draws his actions and gestures, and, whatever he acts of in a way so determined that I can predict his every move within a second, he acts spontaneously. It is therefore at this moment that the subject’s automatism reveals itself better than ever, and it is for this reason that we began our study by the description of a state which, while being conscious, does not,

    however, present normal consciousness or intelligence. Before examining the nature of this rudimentary consciousness, one must take into account a possible objection. Today that it is the fashion to explain everything by suggestion, as formerly by simulation, one could say that all these psychological characteristics of catalepsy have been learned by the subject who has been trained in this direction. It would be dangerous to take this reasoning to the extreme, which would quickly become a sort of “lazy argument” too.

    but it is fair to take this into account; for, doubtless very often, in environments where the subjects are numerous and imitate one another, certain real states in one subject may have been artificial in the second.

    but, for the cases in question here, we will notice that the subjects did not know each other in any way and that the operators should not,

    however, be assumed to be sufficiently naive to have suggested without knowing it all these positive characters. and catalepsy negatives. Besides, an artificial state is always recognized by some sign, and the following observation will perhaps be able to demonstrate it. I had a few experiences with Lucie one day and a foreign person ”was present: I was very displeased with this last circumstance, for you should keep with you only the indispensable persons accustomed to the attitude which one must have during experiments of this kind. This foreign person kept asking me very embarrassing questions; because, as usual, I did not want to answer in front of the subject;

    however, an unfortunate word escaped me: “What is catalepsy?” we asked. “-” It is a state where the subject remains motionless and leaves the limbs in the position in which they are placed. No sooner had I said these words than I felt regret: “From now on, I thought, it will be fair to say that she has suggested catalepsy,” and I wanted to check it out straight away. effect of my recklessness. “Hold on,” I said aloud, “when I clap my hands, she will fall into catalepsy. I knock and here Lucie remains completely still, her eyes wide open: I lift her arms, they stay in the air, I tilt her body, it remains tilted. Was she in catalepsy? It was easy for me to verify that no other sign of catalepsy, nor the expression of physiognomy, nor imitation, nor echolalia could be observed, and above all the subject understood speech so well that To finish the affair I had only to tell him: “It’s over, you’re no longer in catalepsy.” " Well ! that we try to stop a real attack of catalepsy, as Lucie herself had had,

    but very rarely, by simply telling the subject that it is over, and we will see what difference there is between this state of suggestive docility, form of the little hypnotic sleep, and the veritable cataleptic attack, during which the thought is brought back to a quite rudimentary state and which is one of the forms of the great hystero-epileptic attack. I therefore cannot believe that the condition which has just been described is completely artificial. Because it was recognized that it was a conscious state, one should not conclude from it that it is some psychological state some. There are very important differences and varieties even between conscious phenomena. IV A RUDIMENTARY FORM OF CONSCIOUSNESS. - ISOLATED SENSATION AND IMAGE Certain philosophers, like the Cartesians, have represented consciousness as something invariable and immutable without nuances and without degrees. For Descartes, thought existed complete with doubt, reflection, reasoning and language, or else did not exist at all and was replaced by pure and simple mechanism, by extension and movement. Leibniz, on the contrary, in this profound philosophy to which today all the physical and moral sciences seem to bring us back, had a completely different conception of consciousness. He admitted an infinite number of degrees and some of these forms seemed to him so inferior to normal thought “that human minds were like little gods at the cost of them.” It is this last theory that we must now recall in its main features, in order to understand the possibility of lower and rudimentary consciousnesses. Let us take human consciousness in its ordinary and completed form and successively remove from it all the improvements which it has acquired

    but which are not essential to it. Everyone recognizes that it is necessary to separate from the vulgar conscience scientific intelligence, that faculty, which exists in all men to a greater or lesser degree, of explaining and understanding things. This intelligence proper has the result of bringing together [comprehendere) a large number of facts of consciousness into general syntheses or ideas; it arrived there by the discovery of the relations between particular facts, which, being able to remain the same between different terms, give unity to things apparently very distinct. This is evidently the highest term human thought reaches;

    but it is essential to notice that it does not always reach it; perhaps even human thought does it seldom and less often than we are inclined to believe. Indeed, when we examine the actions of other men, we are too inclined to attribute to them the ideas and reasoning that we ourselves make to interpret their behavior. Very often we believe that a man has acted with intention, that he has calculated the consequences of his actions, that he has made of his ideas a systematic whole linked by well understood relations, while in reality this individual has let his thoughts mechanically evoke one another without having grasped any systematic relationship between them. He should not confuse the law or the interpretation of the facts of consciousness as our intelligence finds it, or believes to find it, with consciousness itself. If the phenomena of consciousness presented by a man appear to us to be linked to each other by relationships of resemblance, difference or finality, we must not conclude from this that there was in the mind of this man the consciousness of resemblance. , of difference or of finality ^ It is into this error that the English philosophers seem to fall when they say that all consciousness is perception 1 Leibniz. Erdm., 125, a. ■ ^ See, on this subject, Rabier. Philosophy course, I, 74 and I, 2.5i. of a difference. It is also an exaggeration of this kind that I would sometimes reproach the so interesting works of M. Paulhan, which seems to lend to elementary consciousness the notions of finality which it uses itself to interpret them. Consciousness can exist without any judgment, that is to say without intelligence; man can feel and not understand his own sensations. To take an example in the subject that concerns us: if we are in the presence of the complicated acts that Léonie accomplishes when I put her hands together, we will think that she is making Communion and we will unite all these acts. in this systematic idea which we designate by the word “communion”;
