    but it is not entirely proven that she herself has the idea of ​​communion and that she unites her actions under this general idea; it is much more likely that she has images that evoke each other, and nothing pleasurable. If later she recalls these sensations, which is possible, as we have said, she can examine them, relate them and understand them. “Here,” she said one day in a circumstance like this, “my hands were like this, I was up, then on my knees,

    but I was saying my prayer that I was so stupid. She is surprised now at actions which did not surprise her earlier, because she felt them without understanding them. Below intelligent judgment I would place the phenomenon known as perception. Knowing, when you open your eyes, that there is a tree in front of you of such and such a color, of such a shape, at such a distance, is, as has often been demonstrated, a rather complicated psychological phenomenon. The current sensations, which are already numerous, must be combined with a large number of interpretative images which allow us to appreciate the exteriority, the form, the dimension, the place of the object. These interpretative images which here accompany the main sensation are incidental; they can be modified, as we see in the errors of the senses; they may even disappear, while the sensation persists. Animals whose brains have been removed seem to be in this case; they still seem to hear and see, * Bro. Paulhan. Associationism and psychic synthesis. Philosophical Review, 1888, I, 32, et la. Finality as a property of psychic elements id. 1888, II, 105.

    but they no longer know how to interpret their sensations, that is to say to add to them by themselves the notions of distance, of fear, of desire, etc. The element of consciousness which subsists when we subtract the incidental facts therefore seems here to be the sensation or the image. However, it seems to us that, in order to understand the facts that we have exposed and especially those that we will study later, it is necessary to go still further in this analysis of the elements of consciousness. Sensation is usually defined as “the simple phenomenon which takes place in me when I see, when I hear, etc.” This definition could not perhaps be replaced by a better one, because we define things only by explaining them and by mixing our interpretations with them;

    but it is evident that it contains one term too many: it is the word me, the word I. “It is certain,” said Reid, “that there is no hmma in the a universe which can conceive or believe that odor exists in itself without a spirit or any subject which has the faculty of smelling. "” Can anything happen in the soul, said Garnier, “without knowing it?” If we take a metaphysical point of view, like these authors, if we seek the origin, the cause of sensation, perhaps we will think, like them, that there is no sensation without a soul to produce and know it.

    but, if we take an exclusively psychological point of view, if we consider the ego no longer as a being and a cause,

    but as a certain idea which accompanies most psychological phenomena, we will be forced to think that there are sensations without me, that there can be phenomena of vision, although

    however no one says: “I see”. The idea of ​​the ego, in fact, is a very complicated psychological phenomenon which includes memories of past actions, the notion of our situation, of our powers, of our body, of our very name, which, bringing together all these scattered ideas, plays a big role in the knowledge of the personality. If one considers a simple sensation, it does not contain any of these and on its own is not enough to form such a complex idea. ‘Soarj. Functions of the brain, ISSd, 46et sq. - Mosso.     but we can conceive that certain beings, like the lower animals, can never free their personality from these elementary sensations, and that other more complex beings are momentarily reduced to a purely affective life without knowledge and without reflection. V THE NATURE OF CONSCIOUSNESS DURING CATALEPSY It is precisely a consciousness of this kind, purely affective, reduced to sensations and images, without any of these connections, of these ideas of relation which constitute personality and judgments, which we believe legitimate to suppose during catalepsy and analogous states. Neither the nothingness of consciousness and pure mechanism, nor the knowledge capable of understanding and obeying appear to us plausible here; on the contrary, it is a question of a particular form of consciousness intermediate between these two extremes. Mr. Herzen has just described to us a peculiar state of thought which he observed about himself, the nascent consciousness, so to speak, which manifests itself when the mind awakens after a complete fainting. . If we have insisted on this very curious observation, it is because the cataleptic state seems to us to present great analogies with this nascent state of thought after a syncope. Several authors, such as M. Pitres, MM. Binet and Féré mind awakens after complete blackout. If we have insisted on this very curious observation, it is because the cataleptic state seems to us to present great analogies with this nascent state of thought after a syncope. Several authors, such as M. Pitres, MM. Binet and Féré mind awakens after complete blackout. If we have insisted on this very curious observation, it is because the cataleptic state seems to us to present great analogies with this nascent state of thought after a syncope. Several authors, such as M. Pitres, MM. Binet and Féré, have already reported some sort of syncope during hypnotic sleep,

    but they consider them rare and accidental; I believe, on the contrary, that they are very important and quite frequent, although they are usually so rapid that the observer hardly notices them. In several of the subjects I have studied the cataleptic state was preceded by another state quite analogous to syncope. This is indeed how things go at the onset of Rose’s hypnotic sleep and even in the middle of a sleepwalking session. Animal Magnetism, 118. Suddenly she stops answering and speaking and remains completely still, her eyes closed. If you lift your arms, they fall heavily; if we call it, if we shake it, it does not move. This is not the condition described by Mr. Gharcot under the name of lethargic state, because the pressure of tendons, muscles or nerves does not cause any contractures. It is, it will be said, hysterical sleep which mingles with hypnotic sleep. Without doubt, this state is found exactly the same in the crisis of hysteria and it has, as we shall see, the same consequences.

    but what does it matter, it is nonetheless a real state, and besides, there is not a single fact in hypnotic sleep which does not find its analogue in the crisis of hysteria. In this sort of hypnotic syncope, the organic functions are usually performed in a regular fashion, although there are sometimes, if this state is prolonged, disturbances of respiration;

    but the psychological functions seem to be totally suppressed, or at least I don’t I have never been able to find the slightest sign to tell me of their existence; I therefore have no right to assume consciousness without reason. This state is prolonged more or less long, it sometimes lasts a quarter of an hour or more, it is sometimes so rapid that it is necessary to know the subject well in order to ascertain its existence.

    but after some time there was a change in the subject, although no outward modification was visible. Because if I raise my arms or legs, instead of falling as before, they remain motionless in the position where I put them and continue the movement that I impart to them. However, if I speak, the subject does not react further. Let us wait a few more moments: if I speak now and if I say out loud: “Raise your arm,” opens and repeats like an echo: “Raise your arm. A few moments after this period of echolalia, the subject no longer repeats the commands,

    but he executes them, he actually raises his arm. Another moment and he answers me with increasing liveliness and a consciousness that seems more and more complete. The subject has therefore passed, in this interval of time, from a state where consciousness was zero to another state where consciousness is sufficiently developed for him to speak intelligently. Is it not natural to suppose that it has passed through different increasing degrees of consciousness and, like the cataleptic, then echolalic state, the closest to hypriotic syncope, is it not? legitimate to conclude that these states correspond to the most elementary forms of thought, to the emerging state of impersonal consciousness. Almost all subjects, when they are susceptible to a profound somnambulism, of a true second existence, thus present when they fall asleep a period of transition well known by the old magnetizers, during which they do not show no reaction. We know well the fainting that separates the two existences of FelidaX … ^ and that Mr. Azam called a small death. We find the same phenomena with instructive variations in other subjects. One day, I found Lucie ill, half distraught in that aura state which preceded the great hystero-epileptic seizures in her. I wanted to prevent her from this great crisis, which always lasted several hours, by putting her to sleep immediately,

    but no sooner had I touched her than she suddenly fell into the most complete state of hypnotic syncope or hysterical sleep (whatever the name); she remained there ten minutes without any proceeding being able to provoke the slightest reaction. At this moment, I noticed a curious modification which had just formed; touching his limbs, each time I made a small movement. I noticed that each squeezed muscle, even slightly, contracted immediately and in isolation, then relaxed very quickly. It was possible to study the isolated action of all the muscles of the body on it. It was almost the lethargic state described by M. Gharcot, with the difference that the muscular contraction did not persist in the form of a contraction. A few moments later, the contraction could systematically extend to all the muscles in the arm at once, and the limbs now held the positions I put them in. I opened the subject’s eyes and they remained open, I stood in front of him and he stood up to mimic all of my movements. A few moments later, Lucie began to speak and went into her usual sleepwalking. We will notice here, in addition to the facts previously pointed out, that a state analogous to classical lethargy is interposed between the syncope and the first catalepsy. This disposes me to believe that this lethargy, although it is a real state, is not very well named: it is not a state analogous to death, a “death trance”, and it is not probable. ‘Azam. Hypnotism, double consciousness …, 1887, 66. the nothingness of all consciousness. Besides, natural lethargy does sometimes present general contractures,

    but ordinarily does not bring about this neuromuscular hyperexcitability. Hypnotic lethargy seems to me to be rather an elementary degree of consciousness, a muscular sensation so rudimentary that it remains quite isolated and not generalize not enough to direct the movement of an entire arm ^. A third example will be even clearer. A peculiar feature of Léonie is that any change of state whatsoever is always signaled by a sudden sigh, a sort of small respiratory convulsion. The state of syncope is rare in her, quite accidental, and it has always frightened me, for it is accompanied by respiratory troubles and suffocation. Whether it ceases spontaneously or one precipitates its conclusion by putting the hand on the subject’s forehead, it always ends with a sudden sigh, after which the subject is in a well-known state which is quite the classic hypnotic lethargy with all its characteristics ^.

    but we can notice in this lethargy, more clearly than in Lucie’s, the first return of consciousness. When a contracture has been produced by the shock of the tendons or of the muscles, it is not necessary, as we have already remarked, to strike exactly the antagonistic muscles in order to resolve it; it suffices to strike the muscles at random for other muscular sensations to replace the first. Even more, to undo a contracture of the flexed arm, I just need to gently pull the fingertips. Doesn’t it seem like there ' Gilles de la Tourelle. Hypnotism and Analogous States, 1887, 223. ^ Without wishing to discuss in this purely psychological work the so controversial question of the three states of hypnotism, the importance of which has moreover been greatly exaggerated, I can make a few comments. will notice about the previous observations. Catalepsy and even the state, improperly called lethargy with neuromuscular hyperexcitability, seem to me to be perfectly real states which occur naturally during a great seizure of hystero-epilepsy or even during the state of sleepwalking induced in subjects who have already had seizures like this.

    but it should be added that these states, especially the last one, are very rare, then that they are much more psychological modifications than physical modifications and that the consciousness subsists, although very altered, and even reduced, in lethargy, finally that a very large number of subjects reproduce these states by simple automatism when they have them. heard about it or when they could see them. Cf. I ‘^ part., Ch. or. ‘I have already described this state of Leonie in the following articles in my article on Intermediate Phases of Hypnotism. Scientific review, 1886,1, .577 have some consciousness capable of sensing the extension of the arm, as there is in catalepsy which senses the position of the limbs? The lethargy ends with a sharp sigh and the subject has made further progress. The limbs no longer contract when touched, they understand changes more quickly than they do. we want to print them, they keep the position in which we put them with astonishing precision.

    but the movements communicated do not continue yet, the arms still remaining immobilized in the last situation. I had thought it useful in the past to designate this state which involved lethargy and catalepsy by a particular name.

    but this nomenclature does not actually have much advantage; you can establish as many degrees as you like in this gradual awakening of consciousness. In Leonie these degrees marked by sighs present some distinctness,

    but in Rose the change takes place continuously, and when it takes place slowly, a considerable number of states can be noted between syncope and life. complete sleepwalking. The only thing to remember is is that catalepsy does not present in a single form with the eyes open and with the ability to present simultaneously all the phenomena that I have described. In Léonie, there are three degrees of catalepsy with the eyes closed: first the limbs remain motionless without continuing the movements, then the limbs are able to continue the communicated movements and the figure takes on an expression in harmony with these movements. , finally the feeling of tact seems to be reborn and an object placed in the hands provokes certain usual movements. After this last degree, the eyes open on their own, and there are four forms of catalpsy with the eyes open. I do not insist on the differences of these states which are, I repeat, insignificant or at least very particular on this subject. It’s’ first the sense of sight which wakes up and the subject is susceptible to imitations, then the sense of hearing in an echolalia which is never as perfect as that of Rose, then a beginning of intelligence, of speech and the possibility of inducing hallucinations, then incoherent speech, a sort of delirium, and finally meaningful speech in a complete sleepwalking life. When we approach this last point, the characteristics of catalepsy, imitation, and harmony of expression disappear. This example therefore proves, like the preceding ones, that the cataleptic states correspond to a very rudimentary thought, to sensations quite isolated and incapable of reacting on one another. VI NATURE OF CONSCIOUSNESS DURING STATES ANALOGUE TO CATALEPSY We do not so far we have only studied phenomena artificially obtained, or rather occurring in the midst of more or less artificial states. It is interesting to examine from the same point of view the phenomena which occur naturally; for we must not forget that all these facts have their analogues in a natural although sickly state, I mean in crises of hysteria. Rose’s great nervous crisis presents a development quite identical to that of her somnambulism. After an initial discomfort of varying length, she suddenly collapses. The muscles are flabby, the face pale, no gesture or movement manifests consciousness. Very often this initial syncope, which continues for quite a long time in her, leads to deep and dangerous respiratory disorders. The breathing is sometimes fast and panting, sometimes stops for a whole minute, the lips turn blue and let out a little foam.

    but soon movements begin. They are first small tremulations in each muscle, without overall movements, then movements of the limbs,

    but completely incoordinated. I pass quickly over the details of these convulsions which have been too well described by masters for me to allow myself to dwell on them. I only point out the characters which seem to me to be able to shed light on my subject. It seems to me that the movements, at first quite isolated and incoherent, are becoming more and more general and systematic. For example, at first the muscles of the arm contract randomly, one opposing the other, which simply produces a tremulation of the arm and various flexes of the fingers. Now the muscles agree well enough for both arms to make great movements and punch PIERRE JANET 4 at the same point on the chest. Now, I know that she has at this point, under the left breast, a continuous pain produced, I believe, by a permanent and painful contracture of the intercostal muscles; So I think these arm movements are now coordinated by this painful sensation.

    but, little by little, after this period of convulsions and contractures and mingling with it, for there is no abrupt transition, quite other movements begin. She sits on her bed (she does not get up, because she has both legs contracted even during this seizure), bows, waves with his hands, and smiles at the audience. She was a singer in a concert café and she probably thinks she’s on the stage, because she sings very funny little tunes to us. Or else she probably thinks she is listening to her companions, for she has her hand near her mouth as if to ask for silence, seems to be listening with rapture and from time to time applauds the singer. Lucie presents in her major crises a phase of the same kind that is much more regular still. After the convulsions at the beginning, which last more or less long, the poor girl remains in the posture of terror, her eyes wide open fixed on the curtains of her bedroom. For almost an hour, she does not change her attitude and simply makes a few movements of desperate defense, or utters inarticulate cries. In both of them this period is followed by another in which the development of intelligence seems to be further increased. They no longer obey a fixed idea, they start chatting about things and others. Lucie even has the singular habit of going down to the kitchen and having a basic dinner which she eats with a good appetite, while she refuses to eat when she is awake. This last period of the crisis, which has been called hysterical delirium, corresponds entirely, as we shall see, to sleepwalking: it is therefore not of interest to us now.

    but the intermediate period, that which is designated by the name of the period of passionate attitudes, also occurs. between a state of syncope where consciousness seems to be zero and another state where consciousness is almost complete; it therefore seems to be produced by a still rudimentary consciousness and to correspond to the cataleptic state. Besides, the symptoms are the same: immobility or indefinite continuation of the same movement, harmonious expressions of the whole body, absence of speech as a means of expression and repetition of the same sentence. Finally, which seems decisive to me, we can quite easily switch from one state to another. While Lucie is in artificial catalepsy, I put her hands in the posture of terror; Immediately she remains with her eyes fixed on the curtains and, if I do not hasten to intervene, the rest of the natural vase crisis unfolds for several hours. On the other hand, if I surprise Rose or Lucie in the midst of their natural passionate attitudes, it suffices for me to make a few passes on the arms to put them in so-called artificial catalepsy; because now I can lift the limbs, they will stay in the new position where I place them. This transformation is complete in Lucie who does not take long to forget her terrors and, little by little, enters into her provoked somnambulism; it is only partial with Rose, because there is always a part of the person who continues the crisis, while the other obeys me. We will come back to these complications later. they will stay in the new position where I place them. Other observations confirm these; the attacks of natural catalepsy of Dr. Saint-Bourdin very often turn into a veritable somnambulism like attacks of hysteria. ^ Paul Richer describes the attacks of a hysteria whose limbs can be moved during the passional poses and who keeps them as they are put. These observations suffice to show the analogy which exists between these various states. The great difference which seems to exist between the states which we compare is that, during catalepsy artificial, the origin of the movements and attitudes of the subject is always outside in the modifications communicated to him; during the crisis of hysteria, on the contrary, the The origin of the passionate poses seems to be internal in the memories of the patient. This difference should not be exaggerated: first of all, the internal memories of the subject also play a great part in catalepsy. If I join Leonie’s hands, I make her make her communion,

    but it is thanks to personal memories coming from herself and adding to the feeling of the joined hands. Lucie, who is nothing less than reli- ■ Cf. IP part., Ch. i. ° Saint-Bourdin. Op. Cit., 96. gieuse, does not make his communion and does not even kneel when his hands are joined to him. On the other hand, it does not seem certain to me that external objects play no part in the poses of the hysterical crisis. I read somewhere, unfortunately I cannot find in which book, the description of a hysterical woman who, in her crisis, assumed the postures of the paintings that were in her room. The fact would not surprise me. Lucie always turns her eyes to her curtains, and I have often wondered if she would have the same crisis in a room without curtains. Mary dreams of fire during her crisis, if it occurs during the night, and does not think of the fire if the crisis occurs during the day. It is most likely because at night she sees a lamp on a short distance from her bed.

    but, it will be said, it is very difficult to transform the poses of a hysteric; she does not seem to feel or see you. We saw first that there are exceptions and that we can change the poses of some hysterics in crisis. Then this resistance is explained precisely by the state of consciousness which is so reduced, so small that it focuses on one sensation and is no longer able to feel another. This is why it will be easier to get in touch with the hysteric in the last phase of her crisis * than during this first period. The same fact occurs, moreover, in artificial catalepsy. Anyone can easily chat with Leonie when she is sleepwalking,

    but only I can change her cataleptic attitudes. The arms are, when I touch them, extremely light,

    but they are stiff and contractures for another. We cannot fully explain here this phenomenon of electivity which is rare during catalepsy and to which it will be necessary to return when we speak of systematized sensitivities;

    but it must be observed now, for it shows us that a subject, who is for his magnetizer in a state of artificial catalepsy, is for a stranger like a hysteric in crisis. Luys, “that there are a certain number of epileptics who, in each period of absence, repeat the same acts and utter the same cries or the same words” Le Havre hospital, had a singular habit of this kind: he would sit near a column and make the gesture of ringing the bells at full speed; no one could disturb him from this occupation, which he continued seriously and in silence for nearly half an hour until he woke up completely. This idea of ​​ringing the bells had probably entered into him when he lived in the country and, in that cataleptic state in which he found himself upon awakening from the epileptic coma, she would wake up alone and reign supreme. Many of the states described under the name of exstasis are of the same kind; it suffices to see Leonie motionless, her hands joined and her eyes raised to the sky to understand what the Middle Ages called an ecstatic. The Saint Thérèse, the Saint Hildegarde, the Marie Chantai, the Catherine Emmerich and many others simply had attacks of cata-lepsy, during which the dominant religious ideas or sometimes communicated at the very moment of their attack gave the whole body a harmonious and expressive attitude ^. One takes the pose of the Immaculate Conception ‘'; the other successively takes all the attitudes represented in a way of the cross. Suddenly she stops speaking and her eyes become fixed and motionless, she remains motionless for several hours in the attitude of the most profound contemplation. “Around two o’clock, the ecstatic leans a little forward, rises with a certain slowness, and, as if by a throwing movement, she falls face down on the ground. In this position she is stretched out on the ground, lying on her chest, her head resting on her left arm; the eyes are then closed, the mouth is ajar, the lower limbs are extended in a straight line. At three o’clock, she makes a sudden movement, the upper limbs extend transversely in a cross, the two feet cross, the back of the right foot resting on the sole of the left foot. She remains in this situation until around five o’clock. Ecstasy ends with a frightening scene: the arms fall to the sides of the body, the head tilts on the chest, the eyes close. The face takes on the pallor of death, it is covered with a cold sweat; the hands are frozen, the pulse is imperceptible, she groans This state lasts ten to fifteen minutes; then life wakes up, the heat comes back to life, the pulse is raised, the cheeks are colored;

    but for a few more minutes it is the indefinable expression of ecstasy. Isn’t that a very accurate description of a cataleptic who plays the scene of Christ’s death, instead of simply playing, like Leonie, the scene of communion? Thus, in natural ecstasies, in crises of hysteria, as in artificial catalepsy, we find the same initial fact, an abrupt and complete stop of consciousness which lasts more or less long, which can, “like a dazzling, to have only an elusive duration ^ ”,

    but which still exists. It is at the time of the awakening of consciousness, when this awakening is not too rapid, that ecstasies, passionate poses and catalepsy take place. VII INTERPRETATION OF THE PARTICULAR PHENOMENA OF THE CATALEPSY Guided by previous research on the general nature of consciousness during cataleptic states, let us take up the various phenomena which we have described, that is to say - say the continuation of an attitude or movement, the repetition of movements that have been seen or sounds that have been heard, harmonious expressions of the whole body and associated movements. Let us find out by which simple hypotheses we can interpret each of these facts. 1 ‘^ Continuation of an attitude or a movement. - It is here that we can clearly see the superiority of a real experience,

    however imperfect it may be, on purely theoretical reasoning. Many philosophers, and Condillac especially, have wondered what happens when we introduce an isolated sensation into a statue empty of thoughts. They have assumed a host of things more or less true; they said that this sensation produced attention, memory, pleasure, pain, etc.,

    but they did not guess the main phenomenon that this sensation was going to produce; they did not tell us that with each new sensation the statue would move. The simplest experiment immediately shows us this important phenomenon. Let any sensation produced by any process arise in empty consciousness, and immediately there will be movement. This is the law manifested, we believe, the simplest phenomena of catalepsy. How can we explain, in fact, that the arm of a cataleptic that I raise or that I set in motion retains its attitude or its movement? The physical forces of gravity would tend to make it fall: it takes, in fact, a delicately systematized contraction of all the muscles to maintain it. What can give these contractions their unity and their persistence? I see no other answer than this: it is a lingering sensation. When I raised the arm, I caused a certain conscious muscular sensation, quite determined, that is to say, corresponding exactly to such and such position of the arm, wrist, fingers, etc. This feeling being alone in the mind has encountered no antagonistic and reducing phenomenon, it has not disappeared with the productive excitement, it has subsisted and it still lasts.

    but at the same time as it lasts, it maintains by its very persistence the position of the arm to which it is linked or rather from which it is inseparable. Let us study the different points of this explanation separately. That the position of the arm displaced by me can produce in the subject’s mind a determined muscular sensation which is different for each attitude is an almost indisputable proposition. The skinesthetic sensations, as Bastian says it maintains by its very persistence the position of the arm to which it is linked or rather from which it is inseparable. Let us study the different points of this explanation separately. That the position of the arm displaced by me can produce in the subject’s mind a determined muscular sensation which is different for each attitude is an almost indisputable proposition. The skinesthetic sensations, as Bastian says it maintains by its very persistence the position of the arm to which it is linked or rather from which it is inseparable. Let us study the different points of this explanation separately. That the position of the arm displaced by me can produce in the subject’s mind a determined muscular sensation which is different for each attitude is an almost indisputable proposition. The skinesthetic sensations, as Bastian says, sont peut-être provoquées par le déplacement des muscles, le frottement des surfaces articulaires, les plissements de la peau ou mille autres modifications du membre ; leur origine est encore obscure, mais leur existence et leur précision sont indiscutables. Dans le cas présent, il faut que le sujet ait senti la position de son bras pour pouvoir la maintenir ou la reproduire plus tard, comme nous avons vu que cela a lieu quelquefois. Or, des pré- cautions ayant été prises pour que le sujet ne puisse voir le déplacement de son bras, c’est au moyen du sens kinesthésique qu’il a eu cette sensation. Cette sensation kinesthésiquep eut-elle reproduire ou, dans le cas présent, maintenir l’attitude? C’est là ce qui est plus discuté. On établit d’ordinaire une grande distinction entre les phéno- mènes sensitifs et les phénomènes moteurs. La grande décou- verte de la différence entre les nerfs sensitifs et les nerfs mo- teurs amena la distinction moins certaine (si j’ose avoir une opinion sur ce sujet) des centres sensitifs et des centres moteurs, et celle-ci inspira le désir de trouver dans les phénomènes psy- chologiques une séparation analogue entre les phénomènes de sensibilité et les fonctions ou les phénomènes du mouvement. Dans certains cas, assez rares, il est vrai, on constata dans la conscience les deux phénomènes distincts : je vois un objet qui s’approche de mon œil et je sens le mouvement de ma paupière qui se ferme. Mais, dans la plupart des autres cas, on ne cons- tatait que l’un ou l’autre des deux phénomènes, la cons- cience de la sensation sans la conscience du mouvement, le * Baslian. Le cerveau organe de la pensée, II, 165. sentiment du mouvement sans la notion de la sensation pré- cédente’. On fit alors diverses suppositions : les uns, comme Wundt et M. Gharcot, admirent qu’il y avait toujours une sensa- tion de mouvement coïncidant avec l’émission de la force ner- veuse et précédant tout mouvement ; les autres, comme Bastian, considérant les sensations kinesthésiques comme absolument centripètes, venant exclusivement de l’extérieur, admirent « l’in- conscience absolue de tous les courants centrifuges ^ » ou en général de tous les actes moteurs. Sans préjuger toutes les difficultés que soulève cette question et que nous rencontrerons peut-être plus tard, je crois que le phénomène cataleptique de la conservation des attitudes nous offre un cas simple « préro- gatif » où cette question des rapports entre la sensibilité et le mouvement est plus facile à étudier que dans aucun autre. En effet, nous avons admis un phénomène de sensation à la suite du déplacement du bras. Y a-t-il une raison quelconque pour supposer maintenant un autre phénomène psychologique produisant le mouvement nécessaire pour maintenir l’attitude? Je n’en vois pour ma part aucune, et, d’ailleurs, ce phénomène psychologique que l’on supposerait serait une image motrice déterminée, correspondant exactement à la position du bras qu’il faut maintenir. Ce serait exactement la même chose que l’image précédente déjà produite par la sensation kinesthésique. Pourquoi supposer deux phénomènes qui se confon- draient? Nous devons nous représenter ici les choses g de la manière la plus simple : l’excitation E produit la sensation kinesthésique SK, laquelle suffit pour produire à son tour le mouvement M. Il n’y a pas lieu de supposer d’autres intermédiaires. Dans ce cas simple, il n’y a plus lieu de soulever les difficultés dont parlait Bastian : nous n’avons pas à chercher pj„ i_ si le phénomène moteur a, oui ou non, une cons- cience distincte de celle du phénomène sensitif, puisque les deux phénomènes ne forment qu’une seule et même chose. Quant au troisième point, la persistance de la sensation mus- culaire, il résulte naturellement de nos remarques précédentes. Now, we know that the cataleptic attitude can persist for a very long time; So it is likely, as we said, that the kinesthetic image, encountering no obstacle in this mind which is completely empty, continues until we replace it with another by moving the arm. ‘"’ ‘It is for these muscular sensations that it is right to deduce,’ more perhaps than for any other phenomenon of the mind, that sensation and movement are one and the same thing occurring. in very different aspects, because it is known in very different ways ^ Although in our confused and complex mind this primitive law is often modified, it can be said that regularly and in a simple being there is no movement without a sensation of movement and point of sensation or even an image of movement without movement. 2. Imitation and repetition. - The acts produced by imitation and by repetition will take us a little further in the study of the same problem. Instead of raising the subject’s arm, I show him my raised arm and he puts his own in an identical position. Here, the sensory phenomena (seeing a movement) and the motor phenomena (raising the arm) do not merge as before, and it seems natural to separate them. We can in fact explain things in this way: visual excitation E ‘produced by my movement would bring about the visual sensation SV, this would awaken by association the image’ ^ ‘■ of the kinesthetic sensation SK, which was just now directly awakened, and this image, according to the previous law would bring the movement M to which it corresponds. This explanation ‘See, on this subject, the theories of Lewes in the Psycli. Engl. de Rii> ot, 401, and the works of Dumont. would be quite simple and plausible: it would explain why, in certain cases, the movement by imitation takes quite a long time to produce; it would not raise the difficulties relating to the **consciousness** of motor phenomena, for it always introduces only two phenomena of sensation, only one of which has the property of being inseparably linked, as we do. have shown, to a real movement. If we were to study only cataleptics, we would have no reason to reject this hypothesis;

    but, in anticipation of the difficulties which the study of anesthesias and paralysis will present later, we must remark that the phenomena can, in most cases, be explained also in another way. Could the visual sensation SY not directly produce the movement M without the intervention of any muscular image? These muscular sensations SK could moreover be awakened secondarily as a result of the movement carried out or not be awakened, contribute or not contribute to its improvement and its precision. This hypothesis is first made plausible by certain fairly well-known experiments. We know that any excitation of the senses, whatever it may be, brings about an increase in general force and a disposition to movement which is sometimes expressed by an effective movement. This movement is quite natural, it is the external appearance of visual and acoustic sensation, as the contraction of the muscles was, so to speak, upside down Fig. 3. muscle contraction.

    but if this movement remains general when the sensation is itself vague, like a simple red color or some noise, should it not become precise when the sensation itself is more precise? M. Féré has shown that the sight of a moving object, the sight of a rotating disc, provokes a different motor reaction according to the direction of the rotation ^ Why, in certain cases, the image of would not a determined movement by itself cause another precise movement? * Brother. Sensation and movement, 1887. * Féré. Ibid., 83. This hypothesis is confirmed by research on anesthetic hysterics which we will discuss later. In my opinion, it is impossible to explain how these people can often retain all their movements despite the absolute loss of sensations and even kinesthetic images, if one does not admit that movement can be produced directly by visual images or hearing. Finally, since the fine works of M. Charcot, this hypothesis is universally accepted when it comes to movements of language. From the point of view of language, there are visuals, auditors, motors, that is to say individuals who, in order to represent words, use visual, auditory, joint motor or graphic motor images. These representations play a large role in speech itself and there are individuals who speak with the auditory sense, that is, in whom the auditory image of a word is sufficient to bring about its pronunciation. We can extend this famous theory to all movements and say that certain movements of the arm or the leg can immediately accompany the visual image of that movement without an intermediate kineshesic image. The sight of an arm raising does not resemble the actual movement that it takes to actually raise the arm, nor does the sound of a word resemble the movement of the mouth that it takes to pronounce it. : how can one thing bring the other and merge with it? Note first that we find a similar difference between the muscular sensation of movement and the movement itself. It is the general difference which exists between the physical and the moral, and which prevents ever finding any analogy between a physical phenomenon and a psychological phenomenon, even when their real union is intimate. Then we do not experiment on individuals who have just been born and who have, in their mind and in their body, no association organized in advance. A remark of the same kind will enable us to resolve another difficulty. It will be observed that, in the preceding explanations, we take no account of the phenomena of pleasure and pain to which certain psychologists give such a large role in the formation of movements. For M. Bain in particular, “there is at the beginning of any natural voluntary impulse some of the varied forms that pleasure and suffering take.” For him, “some kind of pleasure or suffering is necessary to give the motor impulse ^”, and the sensations play only an accessory role in directing, specifying the movement and adapting it to the circumstances. We N’ on the contrary, we have not spoken in any way about the phenomena of pleasure or of pain and, in fact, we have not observed anything in our subjects in catalepsy which allows us to suppose these phenomena. This contradiction can very easily be resolved if we consider that we are not studying exactly the same problem as Mr Bain. This author in fact seeks the origin of the activity and its first manifestations in a being who has just been born; we are also studying elementary activity,

    but as it exists in an already formed mind. It may be that early in life movements are determined only by pleasure and pain, because then there is no such thing as other psychological phenomena than those general and vague sensations which are manifested by movements equally vague and indeterminate.

    but little by little the sensations became clearer and the movements with them. The child has learned to feel movement by learning to do it and vice versa. The union which once existed between a vague pleasure and a vague movement exists today between a feeling - ‘Paulhan. Philosophical Review, 1888,1, 45. * A. Bain. Emotions and will. Trad., 1885, 345. ‘Id. Ibid., 342. determined sation and a determined movement, and it suffices that the sensation be brought back even without pleasure and without pain for the movement to take place. We must therefore generalize our previous law and say of every sensation and of every image what we have said of the kinesthetic meaning. An image of movement in consciousness always manifests itself, on the outside, for a foreign witness, by a real movement, and on the other hand this image tends to dare, to persist in his being and consequently brings about the continuation of movement, as long as it has not been replaced by some new image. 3 ° and 4 °. Expressio7is of physiognomy and associated acts. - These phenomena seem more complicated than the previous ones, and it seems that a single persistent image can no longer be sufficient to explain them. With regard to the first sensation, that of a closed fist, of praying hands, etc., there must arise simultaneously and successively a large number of other images which will each bring up. One gesture, the other a facial expression, this one the act of getting up, this one the act of greeting. How is it possible ? Here we see in its simplest form the phenomenon of the association of ideas which is one of the most important manifestations of psychological automatism. Undoubtedly, the images which once occurred at the same time as the sensation caused or as a result now reappear in the same way and in the same order, and it is this automatic succession of images which brings about the regular succession of gestures. and movements.

    but how should we understand this him of the association? Can we not, in some way, bring it back to the previous law of the persistence of a psychological state? We believe it is. Hamilton had already interestingly understood the association of ideas when he said:. “M. Taine likewise considers associations as partial rebirths of totalities which tend to reform themselves completely.” Cf. Ferri. The Psychology of Reassocialion, 1883, 231. menti, m. Paulhan, in articles which we have already quoted, also tries to relate association to the act of synthesis considered as the general function of the mind. These theories strike us, at least in part, very accurate and easily apply to the facts we are studying. The sensation of the closed fist or of the joined hands, at the same time that it is by itself a whole, a complete sensation, was formerly united with a great number of other simultaneous or successive sensations, and was part of a synthesis, of a whole which was the state of anger or the act of communion. It may be supposed with some probability that this set of very different sensations which the mind experienced during the act of communion formed a common feeling, a particular coesthesia which is not the same as in a state of anger or of cheerfulness. By now provoking the sensation of clasped hands, I also awaken, or rather I begin to awaken, this general feeling which existed during the act of communion. This feeling then becomes a sensation like the others which tends to manifest itself and to last.

    but for this feeling to persist, it is not enough that the sensation of the joined hands remain alone in the mind, it would only be the beginning of the feeling. It is necessary, for it to last, that it completes itself and the other constituent sensations reappear one after the other in the form of images and bring about the expressions and movements which correspond to them. In order to fully understand this general explanation of associated acts and in order to be able to use it later, it is necessary to make a few more remarks. One is too disposed, it was a little to the fault of Hamilton and, if I am not mistaken, of Mr. Paulhan himself, to consider this general feeling, this coesthesia as an idea, a real knowledge, to assimilate it. to a judgment or to an abstract idea of ​​finality. True knowledge, judgment, general ideas should not be mixed up with these automatic phenomena of rudimentary thought; they bring with them means of emancipation and a relative freedom of which we see no sign here. This co-anesthesia seems to me much more like a sensitive, conscious image,

    but not ‘Taine. Intelligence, 1, 144. understood, assimilated to a vague religious emotion rather than to an idea of ​​prayer or communion. Emotions are precisely this set of diverse sensations coming from all points of the body: “the special action of the muscles is not only the sign of passion, it is really an essential part of it; if, at the moment when the features express one passion, we try to give birth to another, we will not succeed ‘. The emotions designated by language under the name of fear, anger, love, etc., are few and not very precise;

    but their varieties must in reality be innumerable and correspond in each individual to a determined set of images and movements. It is one of those very precise emotions that we arouse in cataleptics and which brings about their associated expressions and acts. Another important remark is that during catalepsy we can only provoke old emotions already experienced by the subject and we cannot teach him to experience new ones. A subject who is not religious and who has not previously made this synthesis of movements which constitutes the emotion of prayer will not play the scene of prayer during catalepsy. The hands will remain against each other,

    but other acts will not follow. The automatism does not create new syntheses, it is only the manifestation of syntheses which have already been organized at a time when the mind was more powerful. We had previously noticed that simple cataleptic acts did not explain to us the true origin of the activity,

    but only showed us the manifestation of a sensation already formed, so more complex cataleptic acts only show us the manifestation of an already organized emotion. Finally, let us make a last remark which we will have to recall later: these emotions, these associations of ideas can exist, like the sensations themselves, in a rudimentary consciousness like the one we have described. Now, the character of this consciousness, we said, is to be impersonal, not to provoke the idea of ​​the ego or the personality. The association of ideas is therefore not necessarily linked with the formation of the personality, and one can develop without the other. We ‘Maudsley. See Bath. Mind and body, up to now we have seen only the simplest automatic association, which suffices to explain all the phenomena presented by the subjects in the states which we have just described. CONCLUSION In summary, in our normal thought, phenomena are always very numerous and very complex; they collide and change each other, so it is not easy to see their true nature and the laws that govern them. We have studied an unhealthy state in which the phenomena of thought appear, on the contrary, to be almost isolated. One of the best expressions which can characterize this state has been proposed by M. Ochorowicz ‘. Catalepsy was, he said, a state of monoidism. “Certain subjects, capable of presenting these two opposite phases of aidia (hypnotic syncope) and of polyidea (somnambulism), do not pass directly, or at least may not pass directly from one to the other; they stop for a longer or shorter time in the monoideal phase … It is a brain which concentrates all its action on a single, single dominant idea, which is not counterbalanced by any other. “A well-known comparison can still explain these phenomena:” The brain can be compared to a room furnished with an immense number of gas lamps,

    but lit only by a relatively small and relatively constant number of lighted beaks which are not always the same, on the contrary, which change at every moment. As some go out, others come back on. They are never all lit, from time to time they will all be extinguished ^ ”And furthermore, we will add, there are times when only one is lit. Undoubtedly, the importance of this expression of monoidism should not be exaggerated; in the first place, it is more a question of sensations than of ideas properly so called; moreover, these sensations, except in the simplest experience of the beginning, are not reduced to unity.

    but what is true is first that the initial sensation which brings the other images is unique, then that each image remains isolated without • Mental suggestion, Here are the three main laws to which these isolated phenomena have always seemed to us to be subject: ‘P a large number of sensations and images (the preceding studies do not yet allow us to say all) are accompanied by bodily movement and cannot not exist without producing it; 2 ° any sensation or image excited in the consciousness lasts and persists as long as it has not been effaced by another phenomenon; S ' Here are the three main laws to which these isolated phenomena have always seemed to us to be subject: ‘P a large number of sensations and images (the preceding studies do not yet allow us to say all) are accompanied by bodily movement and cannot not exist without producing it; 2 ° any sensation or image excited in the consciousness lasts and persists as long as it has not been effaced by another phenomenon; S ‘every sensation or emotion tends to develop, to complete itself, always manifesting itself by the movements and acts from which it is inseparable. Thus is verified by experimentation one of the most fruitful ideas of one of our philosophers. “Every idea is an image, an interior representation of the act. Now, the representation of an act, that is to say of a set of movements, is its first moment, the beginning, and is thus itself the action begun, the movement both nascent and repressed. The idea of ​​a possible action is therefore a real tendency, that is to say a power already acting and not a purely abstract possibility. »‘Searched. Liberté el determinisme, 1884, 3. CHAPTER II OBSERVATION AND THE VARIOUS SUCCESSIVE PSYCHOLOGICAL EXISTENCES This n ' is that in very rare and extraordinary circumstances that human actions are so isolated and impersonal; they usually seem to be the manifestation of a character and to depend on a personality. In order to continue the study of automatic actions under more complex conditions which approach the normal state, we must seek psychological states susceptible of experimentation,

    but in which,

    however, character and personality begin to develop. to develop: it is the condition known as somnmnbulism which best fulfills these conditions. We see, in fact, in somnambulists, the automatic life of the spirit growing and expanding, forming a particular memory, giving birth to a new character and personality. To discover the nature and the main characteristics of this new form of psychological life is to see the activity of the elements of our thought in a different light. I THE DIFFERENT CHARACTERS THAT HAVE BEEN PROPOSED TO RECOGNIZE SOMNAMBULISM This state is apparently very well known; sometimes, as we know, it occurs spontaneously in the middle of sleep, sometimes it forms an important part of a nervous crisis, sometimes it is artificially provoked by processes which have been too often described for me to insist. However, it is very difficult to find a sign which characterizes it in a general way, and most of the characters which have been thus designated seem to us insufficient; by reviewing them, we will see some incidental characters of this state,

    but we will have to look for the distinctive sign of it. Some of these characters have been called physical, not because they were true visible modifications,

    but because they were observed by means of various experiments made on the subject’s body. Most of the ancient magnetizers considered the insensitive - absolute equality of the skin as being the constant rule and the unmistakable sign of somnambulism. “So that there is somnambulism,” said of Lausanne absolute insensitivity of the skin as being the constant rule and the unmistakable sign of somnambulism. “So that there is somnambulism,” said of Lausanne absolute insensitivity of the skin as being the constant rule and the unmistakable sign of somnambulism. “So that there is somnambulism,” said of Lausanne, il faut que les sens extérieurs n’oc- casionnent aucune distraction et ne sentent rien. » « Il n’y a pas de sommeil magnétique, écrit Baragnon% sans insensibilité com- plète du corps et des sens, de telle sorte que nous nous aiderons, pour la constatation du sommeil, de tout ce qui peut nous con- vaincre de cette insensibilité. » Aussi les magnétiseurs ne man- quent-ils pas d’essayer sur leur sujet des brûlures et des piqûres d’épingles dès qu’il commence à dormir ^ Dans le célèbre rap- port présenté à l’Académie de médecine en 1837; M.Dubois (d’Amiens) se plaint qu’on ne lui ait laissé faire pour vérifier le somnambulisme « qu’un simple tatouage à coups d’épingle sur la figure et sur les mains ». Il aurait voulu faire mieux. Eh bien, le procédé de M. Dubois n’aurait pas grand résultat si on l’appliquait aux somnambules que j’ai étudiées. La plupart de ces personnes, presque toutes, étaient déjà anesthésiques sur une partie plus ou moins considérable du corps avant tout sommeil hypnotique, dans leur état le plus normal. En outre, elles étaient loin d’être régulièrement anesthésiques en somnam- bulisme; au contraire, j’ai été amené, pour certaines d’entre elles et dans certains cas, à considérer le retour de la sensibilité comme une preuve du somnambulisme le plus profond. Un autre caractère curieux est signalé par les mêmes auteurs, ' De Lausanne. Principes et procédés du magnélismeani’mal,]&i9, II,54; de même dans Teste, Magnétisme animal expliqué, ISib, 285. “Baragnon. Etide pratique du magnétiSme animal, 1853, 33. ' Lafontiine. Vurt de magné User, 1860, 99. quoique plus rarement : c’est l’absence complète de déglutition pendant certains états somnambuliques ^ Ce détail m’a frappé, car, chez une personne, chez Léonie, il est absolument constant. Elle n’a aucune déglutition pendant le somnambulisme et jamais je ne suis arrivé à lui faire avaler une goutte d’eau. Dans un article paru récemment, M. Dufay signale le même fait chez une de ses somnambules ^. Mais le phénomène, loin d’être caractéris- tique, est assez rare ; la plupart des somnambules mangent et boivent sans aucune gêne dans leur sommeil. Lucie en somnam- bulisme naturel descendait se faire cuire une côtelette et la man- geait très bien. Rose n’était jamais aussi heureuse que lorsqu’elle déjeunait en somnambulisme. Il y a même des hystériques dys- phagiques à l’état de veille, qui mangent assez facilement quand elles dorment, et c’est un détail qu’il est quelquefois utile de connaître. Mon frère a réussi à alimenter ainsi une femme hystérique qui, à cause de vomissements incoercibles, était près de mourir de faim. Un des auteurs modernes qui ont le mieux connu les som- nambules, M. Despine, croit trouver dans leur attitude extérieure un bon caractère distinctif. La croyance populaire se représente, en général, les somnambules comme des personnes qui parlent en ayant les yeux fermés. Cette croyance résulte probablement de cette idée, en réalité assez fausse, que le somnambulisme est un sommeil : on répète aux somnambules qu’elles dorment, d’où elles concluent qu’elles doivent avoir les yeux fermés. Mais si on laisse les somnambules agir à leur guise, beaucoup, comme Lucie, ont presque constamment les yeux ouverts. C’est alors que M. Despine prétend que leur regard a toujours un caractère tout particulier et distinctif. « Les yeux, dit-il, sont grandement ouverts:..; les pupilles largement dilatées restent immobiles à l’action de la lumière ; la conjonctive insensible ne sent pas le besoin d’être lubréfîée par les larmes, aussi le clignotement des paupières est supprimé ou fort rare ‘^. » L’auteur est si convaincu de l’importance de ce caractère qu’il prétend « par l’inspection des yeux découvrir les fraudes tentées par une fausse somnam- * Baragnon. Magnétisme animal, 108. * Revue scientifique, 1888, II, 241, ^Despine. Somnambulisme, 107. bule ». Il faut avouer que je n’aurais pas une pareille hardiesse ni une pareille conviction. Sans doute, ce regard existe quelquefois, et M. Despine indique très bien dans quelle circonstance, « lorsque la rétine est para- lysée » ; alors, en effet, « ce regard amaurotique a assez de res- semblance avec celui de l’individu qui est assez myope pour ne pouvoir distinguer aucun des objets environnants ». Ainsi, pen- dant la catalepsie, quand on n’excite pas le sens visuel, l’œil prend ce caractère. Si on ouvre de force les yeux d’une hysté- rique au début du somnambulisme, à un moment oti, d’ordi- naire (car il peut y avoir des exceptions), elle ne voit pas clair, ses yeux auront l’aspect amaurotique. Mais, est-il donc admis qu’une somnambule ait toujours la rétine paralysée et soit tou- jours aveugle ? D’après une opinion assez ancienne, que Maine de Biran lui-même a soutenue, le somnambule se conduirait toujours d’après ses rêves, d’après des hallucinations qui lui représentent les objets tels qull les connaît et non d’après de véritables sen- sations visuelles. Cette opinion me paraît complètement inexacte. Si on laisse le somnambulisme se développer suffisamment, il y a des sujets qui ouvrent les yeux d’eux-mêmes, ou bien on peut les leur faire ouvrir, en vérifiant le moment où ils voient clair. On reconnaît évidemment qu’une somnambule se dirige alors d’après la vue des objets réels, comme on peut facilement le vérifier en la menant dans un endroit qu’elle ne connaît pas. Les yeux n’ont plus alors cet aspect bizarre, ils sont tout à fait normaux et, même pendant la catalepsie, si l’on fait fixer un objet, dans les actes par imitation, par exemple, on voit les yeux remuer et prendre une apparence normale. Pour ce qui est de cette attitude des somnambules, j’ai fait plusieurs fois une expé- rience que je crois décisive. J’ai envoyé plusieurs fois Lucie, en plein somnambulisme, parler à des personnes étrangères qui n’étaient pas prévenues et elle a toujours été prise pour une personne normale. Marie peut être laissée en somnambulisme dans une salle d’hôpital, sans que les autres malades soupçonnent son état. Sans doute, il y a, pour moi qui les connais bien, quel- ques traits caractéristiques et je n’aurais pas toujours besoin d’interroger leur sensibilité oit leur mémoire pour savoir dans quel état elles se trouvent : Marie est plus pâle en somnambulisme qu’à l’état de veille; Lucie, qui a plusieurs tics au visage quand elle est éveillée, a une figure calme et régulière dans le second état. Mais ce sont des signes individuels et de minime impor- tance qui ne permettent pas de fonder une distinction scientifique. Enfin pour terminer cette énumération des signes physiques qui ont été proposés, M. Gharcot, puis MM. Paul Richer, Binet, Féré, etc., ont vu chez les hystériques mises en somnambulisme une contracture particulière qui se produisait dans les muscles à la suite d’une friction superficielle ou même d’une simple insufflation sur la peau. Puisque, dans ce travail, je me tiens sur- tout au récit de ce que j’ai pu voir, je dois dire que j’ai cherché ce caractère sur une douzaine de personnes mises en somnam- bulisme et toutes hystériques, et que je ne l’ai constaté que sur deux sujets. D’autre part, une hystérique. Rose, qui ne présente jamais ce caractère en somnambulisme, le présente quelquefois à l’état de veille. C’est assez pour que, au moins pour ma part, je ne considère pas ce fait comme caractéristique. En réalité, j’en suis maintenant convaincu, il n’y a pas de signe physique qui permette de reconnaitre si une femme est en somnambulisme ou si elle n’y est pas, et c’est s’avancer beaucoup que de prétendre reconnaître cet état au premier coup d’œil. M. Despine soutenait que la psychologie n’a pas à s’occuper du somnambulisme ' et que la physiologie seule peut l’expliquer. Eh bien, loin de pouvoir l’expliquer, la physiologie ne peut même pas le reconnaître. Beaucoup d’auteurs, comme Bertrand, Braid et plus récemment Gurney, Bernheim, ont eu le grand mérite de reconnaître que le somnambulisme est un phénomène psycholo- gique et qu’il ne peut être constaté que par des caractères uni- quement psychiques. Cependant tous les phénomènes mentaux qui ont été allégués n’ont pas une égale importance. Le D’’ Carpenter parle de l’état de distraction du sujet hypno- tisé. Il compare son état à la rêverie d’un poète devant un beau passage ou à la distraction d’un savant absorbé par la recherche d’un problème ^. Cela est vague et inexact. Il y a des somnam- bules fort peu distraits et qui étudient un problème avec la plus grande attention. D’ailleurs Stanley-Hall a pu dire au contraire que l’hypnose est une crampe de l’attention sur un objet. Gurney, * Despine. Op. cit., 80. " Voir Gurney. Proceedings S. P. R., II, 266. qui cite cet auteur, remarque, avec justesse, que l’hypnotisé peut avoir une série de crampes de l’attention sur différents objets sans se réveiller en passant de l’un à l’autre. J’ajouterai que les somnambules ne sont pas toujours attentifs, pas plus qu’ils ne sont toujours distraits. Léonie, quand on exige d’elle de l’atten- tion pour des expériences délicates, demande de temps en temps un peu de récréation pour se reposer et pour s’amuser. Bertrand, Braid et surtout Bernheim ont cherché dans l’état de l’activité ou de la volonté la caractéristique du somnambu- lisme et ont constaté que le somnambule n’a pas de volonté per- sonnelle, de spontanéité active et qu’il obéit à tous les ordres. Sans étudier en ce moment le phénomène de la suggestion qui est bien l’un des plus importants de cet état, je remarquerai seulement que rien n’est plus variable que l’état de la volonté dans le somnambulisme aussi bien que dans la veille. Une des plus curieuses études que j’aie pu faire, et sur laquelle je revien- drai longuement, est celle d’une jeune fille de seize ans presque idiote et probablement épileptique. Elle était, pendant la veille la plus normale et durant toute sa vie, plus suggestible, plus hallucinable que la plus docile des somnambules. D’ailleurs, M. Bernheim a parfaitement constaté les suggestions à l’état de veille et a seulement admis que ce phénomène était plus accen- tué en somnambulisme. Mais commentexpliquer alors ces sujets qui, comme Rose, comme Lucie et bien d’autres, deviennent de plus en plus indépendants à mesure que le somnambulisme augmente de profondeur, et arrivent à un état où leur volonté est parfaitement normale, plus spontanée et plus indépendante qu’à l’état de veille . En réalité, la volonté me paraît un phé- nomène secondaire dépendant de plusieurs autres choses, et c’est dans des faits plus élémentaires qu’il faut chercher les signes distinctifs du somnambulisme, c’est dans l’état de la mé- moire et de la sensation. * Voir ch. III de la présente partie. II CARACTÈRES ESSENTIELS DU SOMNAMBULISME : L OUBLI AU REVEIL ET LA MÉMOIRE ALTERNANTE Les phénomènes de la mémoire sont peut-être les plus impor- tants de notre organisation psychologique, et leurs plus légères modifications ont sur toute notre vie un retentissement considé- rable. Or, dans toute la pathologie mentale, il n’y a pas de mo- dification de la mémoire plus complexe et en même temps plus régulière que celle de la mémoire du somnambule. On constate en effet régulièrement dans la pensée des individus qui, pour une raison ou pour une autre, ont eu des périodes de somnam- bulisme, trois caractères ou trois lois de la mémoire qui leur sont particuliers: P oubli complet pendant l’état de veille nor- male de tout ce qui s’est passé pendant le somnambulisme ; 2° souvenir complet pendant un somnambulisme nouveau de tout ce qui s’est passé pendant les somnambulismes précédents; 3^ souvenir complet pendant le somnambulisme de tout ce qui s’est passé pendant la veille. La troisième loi présente peut être plus d’exceptions et d’irrégularités que les deux autres, aussi dans cette étude, qui a surtout pour

    but de donner une idée générale du somnambulisme, insisterons-nous un peu moins sur elle. Mais les deux premières, malgré la diversité que présentent toujours des phénomènes aussi complexes, sont si générales et si importantes qu’elles peuvent être considérées comme le signe caractéristique de l’état somnambulique. Le phénomène de l’oubli, au réveil, de tout ce qui s’est passé pendant le somnambulisme est si curieux et si frappant qu’il a été constaté dés les premières études de ce genre. « Lorsqu’il rentre dans l’état naturel, dit Deleuze , il perd le souvenir de toutes les sensations, de toutes les idées qu’il a eues dans l’état de somnambulisme ; tellement que ces deux états semblent aussi étrangers l’un à l’autre que si le somnambule et l’homme éveillé étaient deux êtres différents… Ce caractère seul est cons- tant et distingue essentiellement le somnambulisme. » « L’oubli * Deleuze. Histoire critique du magnétisme animal., 1819, I, 187. de tout ce qui s’est passé pendant le sommeil magnétique, écrit aussi Baragnon’, est un effet invariable sans lequel il n’y a point de sommeil. » Braid caractérise aussi le somnambulisme par l’oubli au réveil et l’appelle un dédoublement de la cons- cience. Il est inutile de multiplier ces citations que l’on pourrait emprunter à tous les écrivains aussi bien anciens que récents. Il vaut mieux donner quelques exemples pour faire comprendre l’importance du phénomène. Je vais un jour endormir Léonie vers deux heures de l’après-midi, je la tenais déjà en somnam- bulisme depuis quelque temps, quand je reçois une lettre de M. le D’” Gibert qui, ne pouvant venir me rejoindre, me deman- dait de lui amener Léonie. Au lieu de réveiller la somnambule, je lui montre cette lettre et lui propose de venir avec moi comme elle était, a Je le veux bien, répond-elle, mais il faut d’abord m’habiller, vous ne voulez pas que je sorte comme cela. » Elle monte et s’habille ; puis je l’emmène en voiture, ce qui la met au comble de la joie comme un enfant. Elle resta en somnambulisme, pendant toute la soirée, fut très vive et très gaie, se prêta à diverses expériences que nous voulions faire et dans les intervalles raconta une foule de choses. Ce n’est que vers minuit que je la ramène chez elle, et là, à la même place où je l’avais endormie à deux heures, je la réveille complète- ment. Après cette séance agitée, la voici au réveil calme, tran- quille, convaincue quelle n’a pas bougé de la journée et qu’elle vient à peine de s’endormir. Mais, elle reste stupéfaite en voyant qu’elle a changé de costume, et je suis obligé de la rendormir et lui faire diverses suggestions pour l’empêcher de se préoccuper de cette singularité. Autre exemple: Pendant un somnambu- lisme, Lucie s’avise de se fâcher contre moi, je ne sais plus pourquoi. Croyant à une de ces bouderies passagères qui lui sont habituelles, nous ne la surveillons pas assez et elle en pro- fite pour sç sauver au travers des rues en plein somnambulisme. Il fallut la rejoindre, et la forcer à rentrer, ce qui ne fut pas facile. Gomme la scène se prolongeait, je trouvai plus simple de la réveiller. Immédiatement, comme par enchantement, la voici douce et aimable sans le moindre reste de mauvaise humeur et sans songer à reprocher rien à personne. Ghez elle, l’oubli est si com- plet qu’elle ne se souvient même pas, de même que Léonie, d’avoir * Baragnon. Op. cit., 173. dormi quelque temps. Si elle est endormie au milieu d’un acte ou d’une conversation, elle continue presque toujours au réveil son action ou ses paroles comme s’il n’y avait rien eu d’anormal : le somnambulisme, quelle qu’ait été sa durée, semble n’avoir pas existé et les deux moments de la veille paraissent se rejoin- dre. Rose est restée quatre jours et demi en somnambulisme (nous voulions essayer de guérir ainsi une paralysie des jambes qui avait résisté à tous les autres procédés et nous avons d’ail- leurs parfaitement réussi); mais, pendant ces quatre jours, elle parle à plusieurs personnes et reçoit même des visites. Au réveil elle a tout oublié, se trompe sur le jour de la semaine et croit être quatre jours en arrière. Il en est ainsi pour toutes les som- nambules que j’ai pu voir, que leur état anormal soit court ou prolongé, que les événements soient insignifiants ou graves, l’oubli est toujours complet et absolu, c’est une page entière- ment efTacée dans leur vie. Le second phénomène, c’est-à-dire le retour de la mémoire dans un nouveau somnambulisme, est aussi facile à constater. Léonie rendormie, le lendemain de la journée que j’ai racontée, retrouva tout d’un coup l’excitation qu’elle avait eue la veille : < Vous ne vouliez pas me laisser rentrer à pied, me dit-elle de suite, vous avez vu comme je marche bien et j’étais à peine fatiguée. » Lucie, quand je la rendors aussi le lendemain, re- commence immédiatement la scène que le réveil avait inter- rompue. Cette fois, je réussis à la calmer et à obtenir heureuse- ment une réconciliation. Une autre somnambule, N., que j’ai endormi deux fois, à un an d’intervalle, retrouva dans le second somnambulisme le souvenir minutieux de tout ce qu’elle avait fait dans le premier et me rappela des détails que j’avais moi- même complètement oubliés. Tous ceux qui ont endormi plu- sieurs fois la même personne ont remarqué ce phénomène aussi banal que singulier. Le second état possède ordinairement en plus le souvenir complet des actes et des idées de la veille normale : le sujet, pendant le somnambulisme, peut raconter ce qu’il a fait ou senti pendant la journée et connaît encore les mêmes personnes. Une seule fois j’ai assisté à un somnambulisme de Rose, diflërant ac- cidentellement des autres, pendant lequel elle ne me reconnais- sait plus et paraissait avoir oublié la plupart des événements arrivés depuis son séjour à l’hôpital. Mais ce cas est très rare et je ne l’ai point vu se reproduire. Il faudra cependant en tenir eompte si on essaye d’expliquer ces modifications de la mé- moire. Considérer cet état de la mémoire comme le caractère essentiel du somnambulisme, n’est-ce pas se fier à un signe facilement simulable et difficile à bien constater. Nous répondrons d’abord que jusqu’à présent on n’en possède pas de meilleur, ensuite que ce critérium est plus sûr qu’on ne le suppose. Contrairement à l’opinion générale, je considère les phénomènes psychologiques comme bien plus difficiles à simuler que les phénomènes phy- siques, et je crois qu’il serait plus aisé de jouer même une crise d’épilepsie que de feindre la folie pendant plusieurs jours devant un aliéniste. Pour le sujet qui nous occupe, il suffît d’un petit nombre de renseignements et d’un peu d’habitude pour simuler une contracture; il faudrait beaucoup d’intelligence, d’attention et de mémoire pour ne jamais confondre les souvenirs acquis pendant le somnambulisme et les souvenirs acquis pendant la veille et n’être jamais pris en défaut. On peut faire une critique plus grave à la façon de poser les questions pour vérifier l’état de la mémoire. 11 est en effet quelquefois dangereux d’interroger directement le sujet. La question même peut, par une sorte de suggestion, réveiller un souvenir qui resterait sans elle ignoré. Je ne crois pas, en général, ce danger très grand, caries sugges- tions relatives à la mémoire sont rarement aussi faciles, et on ne réveille pas les souvenirs d’un sujet en lui demandant ce qu’il a dit ou fait pendant qu’il dormait. Cependant le danger existe et M. Gurney \ qui l’a souvent signalé, indique aussi un assez bon moyen pour le conjurer : il faut vérifier leurs souvenirs par leur conversation même, sans avoir l’air de les interroger directe- ment. c( Posez-leur une question en apparence indifférente à la- quelle ils répondront d’une manière particuhère s’ils ont certains souvenirs et d’une autre s’ils ne savent de quoi il s’agit. » Ce procédé est excellent et plus facile en pratique qu’il ne paraît être. Mais il faut pour cela, comme je l’ai déjà dit, connaître très bien le caractère et toute la vie du sujet et se résigner à passer un très long temps avec lui. Les expériences psychologiques de- mandent des précautions particulières et ne peuvent pas être ' Gurney. The slarjes of hypnotic memory. Proceedings S. P. R., 1887, 517. faites rapidement. C’est par ce procédé que, au moins toutes les fois que le doute était possible, nous avons vérifié avec soin l’état de la mémoire chez les personnes dont nous parlerons; mais nous n’indiquerons pas ici, pour chacune, les expériences et les conversations qui nous ont servi à faire ces vérifications : cela allongerait inutilement ce travail. Ces disparitions et ces retours de la mémoire existent dans d’autres états que dans le somnambulisme artificiel. On les trouve d’abord et tout aussi nets dans le somnambulisme natu- rel. Un jeune homme, cité par Georget ^ passait subitement après un cri initial dans un état nouveau où il avait un autre caractère qu’à l’état normal, tout en conservant ses facultés. « 11 revenait à lui, si on le serrait à bras le corps, étonné, il avait tout oublié; il retrouvait tout dans l’accès suivant et néanmoins il se croyait dans son état habituel, en sorte que c’était comme deux existences difi’érentes, » « J’ai traité, raconte Erasme Dar- win ^ une demoiselle jeune et très spirituelle affectée d’une rê- verie qui revenait d’un jour à l’autre et durait presque toute la journée; comme elle conservait pendant ses accès des idées de la même espèce que celles qu’elle avait eues le jour précédent et qu’elle ne se rappelait plus l’instant suivant quand il y avait absence d’accès, ses parents s’imaginaient qu’elle avait deux âmes. ï J’ai cité ces deux observations parce qu’elles sont moins connues; mais il est facile d’en recueillir une quantité d’autres. On connaît la malade du D’’ Mesnet qui, une nuit, met tranquille- ment des sous à infuser dans un verre d’eau et écrit qu’elle veat mourir. Elle enferme sa préparation dans une armoire dont elle cache la clef et se réveille. La nuit suivante, l’accès reprend et la voici qui retrouve la clef et court à l’armoire chercher son verre ^ On connaît aussi le rêveur de Despiae qui, toutes les nuits, se vole à lui-même des pièces d’or et va toujours les cacher au même endroit^. Le même fait se constate facilement pendant le délire qui suit la crise d’épilepsie^ et surtout la crise d’hystérie. Rose avait la mauvaise habitude d’injurier régulièrement une servante de l’hôpital à la fm de ses crises. Elle ne s’en souvenait plus après son réveil, et ne pouvait y croire quand on le lui disait. Cepen- dant, à la crise suivante, elle reprenait ses injures au même point et insistait en criant : « J’ai eu bien raison de dire ceci et cela, c’était bien vrai, » et elle répétait tous les détails du délire précédent. Certains auteurs ont montré que la mémoire a un caractère analogue pendant les rêves les plus ordinaires, ce qui justifierait ce mot de Dupotet ^ : « Il n’y a pas de sommeil sans somnambu- lisme. » On trouverait dans le travail de M. Myers de bons exemples, trop longs pour être rapportés ici, où un songe est évi- demment le souvenir d’un autre songe oublié pendant la veille. Ce qui me paraît plus curieux à rappeler et plus utile pour éclaircir ces problèmes de la mémoire, c’est que l’on a constaté des faits analogues pendant l’ivresse d© l’opium ^ et l’ivresse de l’alcool. Les faits sont surtout nets quand il s’agit de l’alcool; chacun sait qu’un homme ivre oublie au réveil ce qu’il a fait pendant l’ivresse. J’ai eu quelquefois l’occasion de faire une petite expérience bien simple : on propose à un individu… trop gai… un bon moyen de prouver qu’il est resté dans son état normal, on lui indique un chiffre et on le prie d’en garder le souvenir pour le répéter le lendemain. En général, si l’ivresse était sérieuse, il serait absolument incapable le lendemain, malgré ses efforts, de retrouver le chiffre qu’on lui a dit. Mais je n’ai pas vérifié le retour de la mémoire dans une ivresse consécutive. Voici une observation des plus nettes à ce sujet. Un nègre com- plètement ivre dérobe des instruments de chirurgie au D’” Keu- lemans®. Le lendemain, il soutient qu’il ne les a pas touchés et * Dufay. Revue scientifique, l” décembre 1885.
